Sufficiency of Cutting Three Slaughter Parts
Explains the four required cuts and the sufficiency of three

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the minimum required cuts for valid slaughter and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Cutting any three of the four slaughter parts suffices; the majority stands in place of the whole.

Slaughter (dhabh) is the means by which animals become lawful for consumption. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on which parts of the throat must be cut, a matter of practical necessity but also of scholarly depth involving the famous principle 'al-ghalib yanubu manab al-kull' (the majority stands in place of the whole). We will explore the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, as well as evidence from the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is slaughter (dhabh). The ruling from this passage is that cutting three of the four specified parts is sufficient for validity.
2
List the four parts to be cut
The four parts are the esophagus (mari'), trachea (halqum), and the two jugular veins (wadajan). These are the minimum areas that must be severed.
The term 'awdaj' collectively refers to these veins.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and evidence
Abu Hanifa holds that any three of the four parts suffice. His evidence is the hadith 'Afr al-awdaj bima shi't' (Sever the jugular veins as you wish), which implies flexibility. He applies the principle that the majority stands in place of the whole.
4
Note the variant opinions of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf initially agreed with Abu Hanifa, but later held that the trachea, esophagus, and one jugular vein are required. Muhammad al-Shaybani requires the majority of each of the four parts.
Malik requires all four, but that is outside the intra-Hanafi scope.
5
Understand the usul principle behind the dispute
The dispute centers on applying 'al-ghalib yanubu manab al-kull' (the majority stands in place of the whole). Abu Hanifa applies it to the total number of parts, while Muhammad applies it to each part individually.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمذبح المريء والحلقوم والودجان وقطع الثلاث كاف. والمذبح إلخ: أي موضع الذبح الذي يستحق قطعه هذه الأربعة لما روي أنه عليها أنه قال: "أفر الأوداج بما شئت"، وهي عروق الحلق في المذبح، والمريء مجرى الطعام والشراب والحلقوم مجرى النفس، والمراد بالأوداج كلها، وأطلق عليه تغليبا. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٣٦/٨] وقال الشافعي: إن قطع الحلقوم والمريء يحل وإن لم يقطع الودجين؛ لأنه لا يبقى حيا بعد قطع الحلقوم والمريء و ما ذكرنا من الحديث. (تكملة، فتح) المريء: على وزن كريم مجرى الطعام والشراب وهو مهموز قال العيني وغير الفراء لا يهمز. (فتح) كاف إلخ: أي قطع الثلاث من هذه الأربعة كاف سواء كانت الثلاثة الحلقوم والمريء وأحد الودحين، أو الودجين وأحدهما، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وهو قول أبي يوسف أولا، وعنه : أنه يشترط قطع الحلقوم والمريء وأحد الودجين؛ لأن كلا الحلقوم والمريء مخالف للآخر، فلابد من قطعهما، وأما الودجان فالمقصود من قطعهما إنهار الدم، من فينوب أحدهما عن الآخر، وعن محمد: أنه لابد من قطع أكثر كل واحد من هذه الأربعة، وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة، وقال مالك: يشترط قطع الكل. وجه الاستدلال لأبي حنيفة ومحمد ما ذكره الزيلعي تبيين الحقائق ٤٥٨/٦] من أن الأكثر يقوم مقام الكل غير أن محمدا اعتبر أكثر كل واحد؛ لأن كل واحد أصل بنفسه؛ لانفصاله عن غيره، ولورود الأمر بفريه، وأبو حنيفة يقول: إن الأكثر يقوم مقام الكل، وأي ثلاث منها قطع فقد قطع الأكثر، وما هو المقصود منه يحصل به، وهو إنهار الدم المسفوح، والتوحية في إخراج الروح؛ لأنه لا يحيى بعد قطع المريء والحلقوم ويخرج الدم بقطع أحد الودجين، فيكتفي بقطع الأكثر أيها كانت، فلا حاجة إلى اشتراط قطع المعين منها، والتوحية التعجيل في إخراج الروح، وحصل هذا جواب أبي يوسف وبقوله: ويخرج الدم بقطع أحد الودجين، جواب مالك من ملا مسكين، فتح بالتقاط)
The place of slaughter is the esophagus, trachea, and two jugular veins, and cutting three is sufficient. 'Al-Madhbah ila kh': meaning the place of slaughter that deserves cutting these four, due to what was narrated that he (peace be upon him) said: 'Sever the jugular veins as you wish,' and they are the veins of the throat in the place of slaughter. The esophagus is the passage of food and drink, the trachea is the passage of breath, and by 'al-awdaj' all of them are meant, and it was applied as a generalization. [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:336] Al-Shafi'i said: Cutting the trachea and esophagus makes it lawful even if the two jugular veins are not cut, because it does not remain alive after cutting the trachea and esophagus, and what we mentioned of the hadith. (Takmila, Fath) 'Al-Mari'': on the scale of karim, the passage of food and drink, and it is pronounced with a hamza; al-Ayni and others besides al-Farra' say it is not pronounced with a hamza. (Fath) 'Kaf ila kh': meaning cutting three of these four is sufficient, whether the three are the trachea, esophagus, and one of the two jugular veins, or the two jugular veins and one of them, and this is according to Abu Hanifa, and it was the first opinion of Abu Yusuf. From him it is also narrated that he requires cutting the trachea, esophagus, and one of the jugular veins, because each of the trachea and esophagus is different from the other, so both must be cut. As for the two jugular veins, the purpose of cutting them is the flowing of blood, so one suffices for the other. From Muhammad: it is necessary to cut the majority of each of these four, and this is a narration from Abu Hanifa. Malik said: cutting all is required. The basis of evidence for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is what al-Zayla'i mentioned in Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 6:458 that the majority stands in place of the whole, except that Muhammad considered the majority of each one because each one is an origin by itself due to its separation from others and due to the command to sever it. Abu Hanifa says: the majority stands in place of the whole, and whichever three are cut, the majority has been cut, and the intended purpose is achieved by that, which is the flowing of spilled blood and speed in extracting the soul; because the animal does not live after cutting the esophagus and trachea, and blood flows by cutting one of the two jugular veins. So cutting the majority suffices whichever they are, so there is no need to require cutting specific ones. 'al-Tawhiya' is speeding in extracting the soul, and this is the answer of Abu Yusuf by his saying: blood flows by cutting one of the jugular veins, and it is the answer to Malik from the poor slave, Fath.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage addresses the required cuts for valid Islamic slaughter (dhabh). It states that the four parts to be cut are: the esophagus (mari'), trachea (halqum), and two jugular veins (wadajan). Cutting three of these four suffices for the slaughter to be lawful. The commentary explains that the term 'awdaj' refers to the throat veins, and the hadith 'Sever the jugular veins as you wish' is cited as proof. A disagreement arises among the schools and within the Hanafi madhhab. Al-Shafi'i holds that cutting only the trachea and esophagus suffices because the animal dies instantly, so the jugular veins are unnecessary. Abu Hanifa's position, also initially held by Abu Yusuf, is that any three of the four parts suffice. Later, Abu Yusuf required cutting the trachea, esophagus, and one jugular vein, reasoning that the trachea and esophagus are distinct and both must be cut, while one jugular vein suffices for blood flow. Muhammad al-Shaybani requires cutting the majority (more than half) of each of the four parts, a view also attributed to Abu Hanifa in one narration. Malik requires cutting all four. The Hanafi evidence, as cited from al-Zayla'i, is that the majority stands in place of the whole. Abu Hanifa argues that cutting any three constitutes the majority, achieving the purposes of blood flow and swift death. This resolves the objections of the opposing schools.

Key Terms
المذبح (al-madhbah): The place of slaughter, specifically the throat area where the four parts are cut.
المريء (al-mari'): The esophagus, the passage for food and drink.
الحلقوم (al-halqum): The trachea, the passage for breath.
الودجان (al-wadajan): The two jugular veins, the major blood vessels in the neck.
إنهار الدم (inhar al-dam): The flowing of blood, a key purpose of slaughter.
التوحية (al-tawhiya): Speeding the extraction of the soul to minimize suffering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, which combination of parts is sufficient for lawful slaughter?
A) Trachea, esophagus, and one jugular vein
B) Any three of the four parts
C) All four parts
D) Majority of each part

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school teaches that cutting any three of the four essential slaughter parts is sufficient for valid dhabh, based on the principle that the majority represents the whole.

Permissible Slaughter Tools and the Exception of Fixed Teeth and Nails
Lists permissible slaughter tools and specifies the exception for fixed teeth and nails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on permissible slaughter tools and identify the specific exception for fixed teeth and nails.

Core Idea
Any sharp object that causes blood flow is permissible; fixed teeth and nails are prohibited.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq outlines the general principle that slaughter is valid with any tool that causes blood to flow, including detached teeth, nails, horns, bones, reed stalks, and stones. However, a critical exception is made for teeth and nails that are still attached (fixed) to their natural places. The ruling is derived from specific hadith evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and list of permissible tools
Read the passage to find the list of objects with which slaughter is permissible: nail, horn, bone, detached tooth, reed stalk, stone, and anything that causes blood flow.
2
Understand the condition of causing blood flow
The key condition is that the tool must be sharp enough to 'anhar al-dam' (cause blood to flow). This is an essential requirement for valid slaughter.
3
Recognize the exception for fixed teeth and nails
The passage explicitly excludes 'fixed tooth and fixed nail' (sinnan wa dhufran qa'imayn). Note that detached ones are permissible.
Fixation means attached to their natural place.
4
Examine the hadith evidence
The general permission is based on the Prophet's command 'Anhir al-dam bima shi't' (Cause blood to flow with whatever you wish). The exception for fixed teeth and nails is based on the hadith of Rafi' ibn Khadij: 'As for the tooth, it is bone; as for the nail, it is the knife of the Abyssinians.'
5
Distinguish between detached and fixed
Detached teeth and nails are permissible because they function as regular sharp tools. Fixed ones are prohibited because they are like bone (tooth) or cause improper slaughter (nail, as used by Abyssinians).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو بظفر وقرن وعظم وسن منزوع وليطة ومروة وما أنهر الدم إلا سنا وظفرا قائمين. وهي قشر القصب أسال غير منزوعين ولو بظفر إلخ متصل بما قبله يعني يجوز الذبح ولو كان بهذه الأشياء. منزوع صفة السن قيد به؛ لأنه إذا لم يكن منزوعا لا يجوز كما يأتي الآن، وكذلك الشرط في الظفر أن يكون منزوعا، وإنما لم يقيده اكتفاء بقوله : إلا" سنا أو ظفرا قائمين [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٢/٢] وليطة: هو على قوله : ظفر، أي يجوز الذبح أيضا بليطة وهي بكسر اللام وسكون الياء: قشر القصب اللاذقة به، والجمع ليط. مروة أيضا بالجر معطوف عليه قطعة من الصخر محددة أي يجوز الذبح بالمروة. وما أنهر الدم أيضا عطف عليه بالجر معطوف أي يجوز الذبح أيضا بكل شيء أنهر أي أسال الدم؛ لقوله عليه "أنهر الدم بما شئت". [رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٢/٢] إلا سنا وظفرا قائمين: أي متصلين بموضعهما ، يعني يجوز الذبح بكل ما أنهر الدم ولا يجوز بالسن والظفر القائمين؛ لقوله عليها في حديث رافع بن خديج: "أما السن فعظم، وأما الظفر فمدى الحبشة"، رواه البخاري، وتأويله إذا كان قائما يدل عليه قوله عليه: "أما الظفر فمدى الحبشة"؛ لأنهم كانوا يذبحون بالقائم. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٢/٢]
And even with a nail, horn, bone, a detached tooth, a reed stalk, a hard stone, and anything that causes blood to flow, except a fixed tooth and a fixed nail. 'Wa liqa' is the peel of reed. 'Asal' means detached, not fixed. 'Wa law bi dhufr ila kh' is connected to what precedes: meaning it is permissible to slaughter even with these things. 'Manzu' is the description of the tooth; it is qualified because if it is not detached, it is not permissible, as will come now. Similarly, the condition for the nail is that it be detached; he did not qualify it, relying on his saying: 'except a fixed tooth or a fixed nail' [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:362]. 'Wa liqa': according to his saying: 'zafr', meaning it is permissible to slaughter also with liqa, which is (with kasr of lam and sukun of ya') the peel of reed next to it, plural 'lita'. 'Marwa' also in the genitive is coordinated with it: a piece of sharpened rock, meaning it is permissible to slaughter with marwa. 'Wa ma anhar al-dam' also in the genitive is coordinated with it: meaning it is permissible to slaughter also with anything that causes blood to flow (i.e., makes it flow), due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'Cause the blood to flow with whatever you wish' [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:362]. Except a fixed tooth and a fixed nail: meaning attached to their places. That is, it is permissible to slaughter with everything that causes blood to flow, and it is not permissible with a fixed tooth and a fixed nail, due to his (peace be upon him) saying in the hadith of Rafi' ibn Khadij: 'As for the tooth, it is bone; as for the nail, it is the knife of the Abyssinians', narrated by al-Bukhari. Its interpretation when it is fixed is indicated by his saying: 'As for the nail, it is the knife of the Abyssinians' because they used to slaughter with the fixed nail. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:362]
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage lists the objects with which slaughter is permissible. It extends the general permissibility to include a detached nail (zafr), horn (qarn), bone ('azm), a detached tooth (sin), a reed stalk (lita), a sharp stone (marwa), and any object that causes blood to flow (anhar al-dam). The key condition is that the object must be sharp and detached from its original place. The exception is a fixed tooth or a fixed nail, which are prohibited because the hadith from Rafi' ibn Khadij states that teeth are bone and fixed nails are like the knives of the Abyssinians, who used them for slaughter in a way that does not ensure proper blood flow and causes unnecessary suffering. The commentary clarifies that 'manzu' (detached) is a required condition for the tooth and nail. For other objects like the reed stalk and stone, no such condition is needed; any sharp edge suffices. The permissibility is based on the prophetic command: 'Cause the blood to flow with whatever you wish.'

Key Terms
ليطة (līṭa): The thin peel of reed that sticks to it, used for slaughter.
مروة (marwa): A sharp piece of rock or stone used for slaughter.
أنهر الدم (anhara al-dam): To cause blood to flow, a necessary condition for slaughtering tool.
قائمين (qa'imayn): Attached/fixed in their place (teeth and nails).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is slaughter with a fixed tooth prohibited?
A) It is not sharp enough
B) It causes excessive pain
C) It is considered bone and does not cause proper blood flow
D) It is only for Abyssinians

Key Takeaway
Slaughter is valid with any sharp object that causes blood to flow, except for fixed teeth and nails.

Recommended and Disliked Acts in Slaughter
Lists recommended and disliked acts in slaughter with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the recommended and disliked acts during slaughter and explain why the meat remains halal despite the dislikes.

Core Idea
Sharpen the blade (recommended); avoid severing the spine, cutting the head, and slaughtering from the back (disliked)—but the meat is lawful.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq with its commentary outlines the sunnah and makruh aspects of slaughter. The matn first states the recommended act, then lists disliked acts. The commentary provides hadith proofs and clarifies that these dislikes do not render the meat haram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended act
The text begins with 'وَ نُدِبَ حَدُّ الشَّفْرَةِ' (it is recommended to sharpen the blade). The commentary cites the hadith commanding ihsan in slaughter.
The hadith is from Muslim and others.
2
List the disliked acts
The matn mentions four: al-nakh' (severing spinal cord), cutting the head, slaughtering from the back of the neck. Commentary adds dragging, skinning before cooling, and facing non-qibla.
3
Understand the reasoning for dislike
The commentary explains that these are disliked due to increased pain (za'id al-alam), not because of inherent prohibition.
The meat is lawful in all these cases.
4
Evaluate the effect on the meat
The text states 'يُؤكَلُ في جَمِيعِ ذَلِكَ' (it is eaten in all that) because the croohah (dislike) does not necessitate hurmah (prohibition).
5
Note the exception for back-of-neck slaughter
Slaughter from the back of the neck is halal only if the animal remains alive and the vessels are cut; if it dies before, it is not eaten.
This is an important conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وندب حد الشفرة، وكره النخع، وقطع الرأس، والذبح من القفا. وذبح صيد قبل الإصجاع وكره بعده عظم الرقبة استأنس، ويجرح نعم توحش أو تردى في بئر. صفة صيد ضربين كالغنم والإبل والبقر أي سقط وندب إلخ: لقوله عليه: 'إن الله كتب الإحسان على كل شيء، فإذا قتلتم فأحسنوا القتلة، وإذا ذبحتم فأحسنوا الذبحة، وليحد أحدكم شفرته وليرح ذبيحته'، رواه مسلم وغيره ويكره أن يضجعها ثم يحد الشفرة؛ لقوله عليا لمن أضجع الشاة وهو يحد شفرته: 'لقد أردت أن تميتها موتتين هلا حددتها قبل أن تضجعها' الحديث، والآلة على قاطعة وغير قاطعة والقاطعة على ضربين حادة ،وكليلة فالحادة اختيارية وضرورية، فالحادة يجوز الذبح بها من غير كراهة والكليلة يجوز الذبح بها، ويكره لما مر من الإبطاء في الإراقة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٣٧/٨] وكره النخع إلخ هو أن يصل إلى النخاع، وهو خيط أبيض في جوف عظم الرقبة، وهو بالفتح، والضم لغة فيه، وإنما كره لورود النهي فيه وقيل هو أن يمد رأسها حتى يظهر مذبحها وقيل أن يكسر رقبتها قبل أن تسكن من الاضطراب وكل ذلك مكروه لا خلاف فيه، وإنما الخلاف في تعيين أحد هذه المعاني في تفسير النخع، وفي قطع الرأس زيادة تعذيب فيكره. ويكره أن يجر ما يريد ذبحه إلى المذبح، وأن يسلخ قبل أن يبرد، ويؤكل في جميع ذلك؛ لأن الكراهة لمعنى زائد، وهو زيادة الألم، فلا يوجب الحرمة. ويكره أن يذبحها موجهة لغير القبلة؛ لمخالفة السنة في توجيهها للقبلة، ولا كراهة في الأكل. وأما الذبح من القفا ففيه زيادة ألم فيكره وتحل إذا بقيت حية حتى تقطع العروق لتحقق الموت بالذكاة، وإن ماتت قبل قطع العروق لا تؤكل لوجود الموت بما ليس بذكاة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٣٧/٨] وذبح إلخ بصيغة الفعل المبني للمجهول والجملة مستأنفة، وقراءته بصيغة المصدر، وجعل الخبر محذوفا تقديره يؤكل تكلف لا داعي إليه، يعني إذا استأنس صيد بأن تألف الظبي ونحوه في البيت يذبح؛ لأنه وإن كانت ذكاته ذكاة الاضطرار؛ إلا أنها لا يصار إليها إلا عند العجز عن ذكاة الاختيار، ولا عجز هنا فلا يصار إليها. رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٣/٢] ويجرح إلخ: لأن النعم وإن كانت ذكاته ذكاة الاختيار إلا أنه لما توحش تحقق العجز عنها، فيصار إلى ذكاة الاضطرار. واعلم أنه لا يشترط للذكاة الاضطرارية تعذر الاختيارية بل يكفي التعسر، ذكره العلامة الشرنبلالي أخذا مما في 'منية المفتي' حيث قال: بعير أو ثور ند في المصر إن علم صاحبه أنه لا يقدر على أخذه إلا أن يجتمع جماعة كثيرة، فله أن يرميه. (فتح بزيادة) أو تردى في بئر إلخ: يعني إذا تردى حيوان في بئر، وحصل العجز عن الوصول إليه ليذبح، فإنه يجرح، ويؤكل إذا علم أن موته من الجرح وإلا لا وإن أشكل ذلك أكل؛ لأن الظاهر أن الموت منه ، وكذا الدجاجة إذا تعلقت على شجرة وخيف موتها صار ذكاتها الجرح، ثم إن الشيخ أطلق الجواب فيما توحش من النعم، وكذا فيما تردى، وعن محمد : أن الشاة إذا ندت في المصر لا تحل بالعقر؛ لأنه يمكن أخذها فلا يتحقق العجز، وإن ندت في الصحراء فتحل بالعقر، وفي الإبل والبقر يتحقق العجز في المصر والصحراء فيحل بالعقر، والصيال كالند،
It is recommended to sharpen the blade, and disliked to sever the spine (nakh'), cut the head, and slaughter from the back of the neck. And a hunted animal that becomes domesticated is slaughtered, and a domestic animal that becomes wild or falls into a well is wounded. The description of hunting is of two types, like sheep, camels, and cattle - i.e., it has fallen. 'It is recommended ila kh' due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'Indeed Allah has prescribed ihsan in everything; so when you kill, kill well; when you slaughter, slaughter well; let one of you sharpen his blade and give rest to his slaughtered animal' narrated by Muslim and others. It is disliked to lay it down and then sharpen the blade, due to his saying to someone who laid down a sheep while sharpening his blade: 'You have intended to give it two deaths; why did you not sharpen it before laying it down?' The tool is either cutting or not cutting; cutting is of two types: sharp and dull. The sharp is optional and required; slaughter with it is permissible without dislike, and with the dull it is permissible but disliked due to the delay in blood flow [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:337]. 'It is disliked to sever the spine (nakh') ila kh' means to reach the spinal cord, which is a white thread in the cavity of the neck bone. It is pronounced with fatha and dammah is a language variation. It is disliked because of the prohibition reported. It is also said to stretch the head until the throat appears, or to break the neck before the animal settles from agitation. All of this is disliked without disagreement; the disagreement is only in specifying one of these meanings for the interpretation of 'nakh''. Cutting the head increases torture so it is disliked. It is disliked to drag the intended slaughter to the slaughter place, and to skin before the body cools. The meat is lawful in all these because the dislike is for an added reason (increased pain), so it does not necessitate prohibition. It is disliked to slaughter facing other than the qibla due to violating the Sunnah of facing the qibla; there is no dislike in eating the meat. As for slaughtering from the back of the neck, there is increased pain so it is disliked, but it is lawful if the animal remains alive until the veins are cut and death is by proper slaughter; if it dies before the veins are cut, it is not eaten because death occurred by what is not valid slaughter [Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:337]. 'Wa dhubiha ila kh' in the passive verb form, the sentence is a new statement; reading it as a masdar with an omitted predicate (i.e., 'it is eaten') is unnecessary effort. Meaning: if a hunted animal becomes domesticated (e.g., a gazelle that becomes tame in the house), it is slaughtered because although its valid slaughter is by emergency slaughter (dharurat), that method is only resorted to in case of inability to perform optional slaughter; there is no inability here, so it is not resorted to [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:363]. 'Wa yujrah ila kh' because domestic animals, although their valid slaughter is by optional method, when they become wild, inability to do so is realized, so emergency slaughter is performed. Know that for emergency slaughter, it is not necessary that optional slaughter be impossible; difficulty is sufficient. This was mentioned by al-'Allama al-Sharnabalali, taking from 'Munyat al-Mufti', where he said: If a camel or bull becomes wild in the city, and its owner knows that he cannot take it except by gathering a large group, he may shoot it. (Fath with addition) 'Aw taradda fi bi'r ila kh' means: if an animal falls into a well and there is inability to reach it to slaughter, then it is wounded; and it is eaten if it is known that death was from the wound; otherwise no. If it is unclear, it is eaten because the apparent is that death is from it. Similarly, a chicken if it becomes entangled in a tree and its death is feared, its valid slaughter becomes wounding. The sheikh gave an absolute answer for domestic animals that become wild, and similarly for those that fall. It is narrated from Muhammad that a sheep that becomes wild in the city cannot be lawful by wounding because it can be caught, so inability is not realized; if it becomes wild in the desert, it becomes lawful by wounding. For camels and cattle, inability is realized in the city and the desert, so wounding makes it lawful. The case of an animal that attacks is like the wild case.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage covers the recommended and disliked practices during slaughter and the rules for animals that become wild or fall into wells. It begins by stating that sharpening the blade is recommended (mandub) based on the hadith commanding ihsan in slaughter. Disliked acts include: severing the spinal cord (nakh'), cutting off the head, slaughtering from the back of the neck, dragging the animal to the slaughter place, skinning before the body cools, and slaughtering facing other than the qibla. These are all makruh tanzihi (disliked) but do not render the meat unlawful because the dislike is due to additional pain, not prohibition. The meat is still halal. Then the passage discusses special cases: if a wild animal (like a gazelle) becomes domesticated, it must be slaughtered by the normal method, not by wounding, because there is no inability to perform proper slaughter. Conversely, if a domestic animal (like a cow) becomes wild and cannot be easily caught, it can be slaughtered by wounding (e.g., shooting) as an emergency measure. Similarly, if an animal falls into a well and cannot be reached, it can be wounded. The key principle is that emergency slaughter is allowed when there is difficulty (ta'assur) or inability to perform normal slaughter. For sheep, the ruling differs based on location: in the city it is usually possible to catch, so wounding is not allowed; in the desert it is allowed. For camels and cattle, wounding is allowed even in the city due to their size. The passage also clarifies that if the animal dies from the wound, it is lawful if it is known that blood flowed from the neck vessels; if uncertain, the default is permissibility.

Key Terms
النخع (al-nakh'): Severing the spinal cord (the white thread in the neck bone), or breaking the neck before the animal settles.
ذكاة الاضطرار (dhabh al-dharurat): Emergency slaughter by wounding when normal slaughter is impossible or extremely difficult.
الاستئناس (al-isti'nas): Domestication of a wild animal; it then requires normal slaughter, not wounding.
التوحش (al-tawahhush): When a domestic animal becomes wild, allowing emergency slaughter by wounding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a person severs the spinal cord (nakh') during slaughter?
A) The meat becomes haram.
B) The slaughter becomes invalid.
C) The meat is halal but the act is disliked.
D) The act is recommended.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafis permit meat from a slaughter that includes disliked acts, as long as the essential conditions are met.

Ruling on Domesticated Wild Animals and Emergency Slaughter
Explains rulings for domesticated wild animals and wild domestic animals with emergency slaughter conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when a wild animal becomes domesticated (requiring normal slaughter) and when a domestic animal becomes wild (permitting emergency slaughter by wounding), and apply the principle of 'difficulty suffices for emergency slaughter.'

Core Idea
If a wild animal becomes tame, slaughter it normally; if a tame animal goes wild, wounding it suffices when normal slaughter is difficult.

This lesson continues the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, focusing on the second part of the matn regarding the transformation of wild and domestic animals. The key principle is that emergency slaughter (dhabh al-dharurah) is allowed when there is difficulty (ta'assur), not only impossibility. The commentary provides details on sheep versus cattle and situations like falling into a well.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The matn states: a hunted animal that becomes domesticated (ista'nas) must be slaughtered (not wounded); a domestic animal that becomes wild (tawahhush) or falls into a well is wounded (not slaughtered).
2
Understand the rationale
For domesticated wild animals: normal slaughter is possible, so emergency slaughter is not allowed. For wild domestic animals: normal slaughter is difficult, so emergency slaughter is allowed.
Difficulty suffices; impossibility is not required.
3
Analyze the ruling for sheep vs. camels/cattle
From Muhammad: a sheep that goes wild in the city is still catchable, so wounding not allowed; in the desert it is allowed. For camels and cattle, wounding is allowed even in the city due to size and difficulty.
This shows the principle of difficulty applied differently.
4
Apply to falling into a well
If an animal falls into a well and cannot be reached, wounding is allowed. The meat is eaten if it is known that death is from the wound; if uncertain, it is presumed that death was from the wound.
The default is permissibility.
5
Note the scope of the ruling
The commentary from the sheikh gives an absolute answer for domestic animals that become wild; but the detail from Muhammad shows that the ruling may differ based on the type of animal and location.
Attention to the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Prohibition of Eating Predators and Birds of Prey
Ruling on prohibited animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating predators and birds of prey, identify the primary dalil, and apply the criterion to new cases.

Core Idea
Every predator with fangs and every bird with talons is haram.

This lesson explores a concise but powerful ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq: the prohibition of consuming predatory animals and birds of prey. The text presents both a hadith from Ibn Abbas and a rationale based on the harmful nature of these creatures. Understanding this ruling will equip you to identify which land animals and birds are impermissible in the Hanafi diet, beyond those explicitly mentioned in the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the key sentence: 'لا يؤكل كل ذي ناب من السباع وكل ذي مخلب من الطيور'. Note that it prohibits two categories: animals with fangs from among predators, and birds with talons. The ruling is general (kull).
2
Cite the primary proof
The text quotes the hadith from Ibn Abbas (narrated by Muslim and others) as the direct proof. Identify the hadith wording: 'كل ذي ناب من السباع وكل ذي مخلب من الطيور'.
This is a sunnah ahad with zanni thubut but qati dalala.
3
Explain the rationale and its implications
The text adds a rationale: these animals have a blameworthy nature that may transmit to humans, so they are prohibited out of honor for humans. Understand that this rationale supports the general application of the ruling.
The rationale is a secondary proof (talil) reinforcing the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يحل أكله وما لا يحل
لا يؤكل لما روي عن ابن عباس علما أنه عليها هي أكل كل ذي ناب من السباع، وكل ذي مخلب من الطيور، رواه مسلم وآخرون ولأن طبيعة هذه الأشياء مذمومة شرعا فيخشى أن يتولد من لحمها شيء من طباعها فتحرم إكراما لبني آدم والسباع جمع سبع، وهو كل مختطف منتهب جارح قاتل عاد عادة، والمراد بذي =
Section on what is halal to eat and what is not halal. It is not eaten due to what is narrated from Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet forbade eating every predator with fangs and every bird with claws, narrated by Muslim and others. And because the nature of these things is reprehensible in Shariah, and it is feared that from their meat something of their nature may be generated, so they are prohibited as a honor for the children of Adam. Predators (siba') are beasts of prey that snatch, plunder, wound, kill habitually. What is meant by 'having fangs' (dhi nāb) =
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This is a brief but important passage from the section on permissible and prohibited foods. It states the general principle: every predator that has fangs (like lions, wolves, etc.) and every bird that has talons (like eagles, hawks) is not permissible to eat. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Abbas. The commentary explains the rationale: these animals have harmful natures that might transfer to humans, so eating them is prohibited as a matter of honor for human beings. The definition of 'sabu' (predator) is given: any animal that habitually snatches, plunders, and kills. The passage appears incomplete in the scan but the ruling is clear.

Key Terms
سباع (sibā'): Predatory animals that habitually snatch, plunder, and kill; specifically those with fangs (nāb).
مخلب (mikhlab): Talons or claws of birds of prey used for hunting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A sheep
B) A chicken
C) A wolf (having fangs)
D) A sparrow

Key Takeaway
The prohibition on eating predators with fangs and birds with talons is based on a general hadith and the harmfulness of their nature, and it applies to all such animals.

Eating Horse Meat: Hanafi Dispute Between Imam and His Disciples
Intra-Hanafi dispute on horse meat with proofs and tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on eating horse meat, identify the proofs for each view, and explain the relied-upon fatwa of makruh tanzih.

Core Idea
The later Hanafi fatwa on horse meat is makruh tanzih, reconciling Abu Hanifa's original prohibition with the Sahibayn's permissibility.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute: the ruling on eating horse meat. While the school is united on most dietary matters, this case reveals a difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two primary disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding how later Hanafi authorities resolved this ikhtilaf provides insight into the school's methodology of tarjih (preferring one position) and the evolution of the mufta bihi (operative ruling).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Hanafi positions
State Abu Hanifa's original view, the view of the Sahibayn, and the later reconciled position. Note that Abu Hanifa reportedly retracted his view before death.
The sources disagree on whether Abu Hanifa's view was tahrim or just karahah; the text mentions he said 'not halal'.
2
Analyze the proofs for each side
For Abu Hanifa: Quran 16:8 and hadith of Khalid ibn al-Walid forbidding horse meat; also the argument that horses are tools of jihad. For the Sahibayn: hadith of Jabir that horse flesh was eaten in the Prophet's time.
The quality of the hadiths is discussed: the forbidding hadith is narrated by Ahmad and Abu Dawud; the permissive hadith is from Jabir.
3
Trace the tarjih process
Later authorities like al-Marghinani (in al-Hidayah) and al-Haskafi (in al-Durr al-Mukhtar) weighed the evidence. The report of Abu Hanifa's retraction shifted the weight to the Sahibayn's view, but with tanzih dislike.
The text quotes several sources: al-Ayni, al-Shami, al-Qahistani, etc.
4
Understand the final ruling and its application
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that horse meat is makruh tanzih (disliked but not sinful). This applies only to land horses; sea horses are unanimously not eaten.
The ruling on milk of horses is also mentioned: 'no harm in its milk'.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
A Hanafi following Abu Hanifa's original view would avoid horse meat entirely; one following the Sahibayn would consider it permissible but makruh tanzih. The mufta bihi means it is better to avoid but not sinful to eat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يحل أكله وما لا يحل
كتاب الذبائح والخيل، وحل الأرنب وذبح ما لا يؤكل يطهر لحمه وجلده إلا الآدمي والخنزير،
والخيل: أي وكذا لا يحل الخيل عند أبي حنيفة وهو قول ابن عباس، وبه قال مالك، وعندهما: يحل؛ لما روى جابر بن عبد الله لهما قال : أكل لحم الفرس على عهد رسول الله ﷺ، وبه قال الشافعي وأحمد، وله: قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْخَيْلَ وَالْبِغَالَ وَالْحَمِيرَ لِتَرْكَبُوهَا وَزِينَةً﴾ (النحل:۸) خرجت الآية مخرج الامتنان، فلو كان حل الأكل ثابتا لم يمن علينا بذلك، ولما روى خالد بن الوليد أن النبي ﷺ نهى عن لحوم الخيل، رواه أحمد، وفي رواية أبي داود أنه سمع النبي يقول: "لا تحل لحوم الخيل [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٥/٢] ولأنه آلة إرهاب العدو، فيكره أكله احتراما بسهم في الغنيمة؛ ولأن في إباحته تقليل آلة الجهاد. له
له، ولهذا يضرب ثم اختلفوا أنه كراهة تنزيه أو تحريم والأصح التحريم، كذا في العيني والهداية، قال في "الدر المختار": وقيل: إن أبا حنيفة رجع عن حرمته قبل موته بثلاثة أيام، وعليه الفتوى كذا في العمادية، ولا بأس بلبنها على الأوجه. قال في "الشامي": فهو مكروه كراهة تنزيه، وهو ظاهر الرواية كما في "كفاية البيهقي"، وهو الصحيح على ما ذكره فخر الإسلام وغيره كذا في "القهستاني"، ثم نقل أي القهستاني تصحيح كراهة التحريم عن "الخلاصة" و"الهداية" و"المحيط" و"المغني" و "قاضي خان" و"العمادي ،وغيرهم وعليه المتون، وأفاد أبو السعود أنه على كراهة التنزيه لا خلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه؛ لأنهما وإن قالا بالحل لكن مع كراهة التنزيه كما صرح به في "الشرنبلالية" عن "البرهان"، والخلاف في خيل البر، أما خيل البحر فلا تؤكل اتفاقا.
What is permissible to eat and what is not. Book of slaughterings and horses, and permissibility of rabbit, and slaughtering what is not eaten purifies its meat and hide except human and pig. And horses: meaning likewise horse is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa, and it is the saying of Ibn Abbas, and Malik said the same; but according to the two disciples it is permissible because Jabir ibn Abdullah narrated for them: 'Horse flesh was eaten during the time of the Messenger of Allah ﷺ, and al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said the same. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is the verse: 'And horses, mules and donkeys to ride and as adornment' (al-Nahl 8) – the verse came in the context of bestowal, so if permissibility of eating had been established, He would not have bestowed it upon us. And because Khalid ibn al-Walid narrated that the Prophet ﷺ forbade eating horse flesh, narrated by Ahmad, and in Abu Dawud's narration that he heard the Prophet say: 'Horse flesh is not halal'. And because it is a tool for intimidating the enemy, so it is disliked to eat it out of respect, and because in its permissibility is reduction of the tool of jihad. Then they differed whether it is Tanzihi or Tahrimi dislike, and the correct is Tahrimi, as in al-Ayni and al-Hidaya. Al-Durr al-Mukhtar said: It is said that Abu Hanifa retracted from its prohibition three days before his death, and the fatwa is upon that, as in al-Imadiyya, and there is no harm in its milk according to the most correct views. Al-Shami said: It is Makruh Tanzihi, and that is the apparent narration as in Kifayat al-Bayhaqi, and it is the correct according to Fakhr al-Islam and others, as in al-Qahistani. Then al-Qahistani reported the correction of Tahrimi dislike from al-Khulasa, al-Hidaya, al-Muhit, al-Mughni, Qadi Khan, al-Imadi and others, and the texts are upon it. Abu al-Su'ud stated that on Tanzihi dislike there is no difference between the Imam and his two companions; because although they said it is permissible, it is with Tanzihi dislike as declared in al-Sharnaballiyya from al-Burhan. And the dispute is about land horses; as for sea horses, they are not eaten by agreement.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of eating horse meat. The author begins by stating the ruling for horse meat: according to Abu Hanifa, it is not halal, and this is also the position of Ibn Abbas and Imam Malik. However, his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, hold it is halal. They rely on a hadith from Jabir that horse flesh was eaten in the Prophet's time, and this is also the opinion of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad. Abu Hanifa's proof is twofold: first, the Quranic verse (al-Nahl 8) which mentions horses for riding and adornment, implying that their primary purpose is not consumption; if eating them were permissible, that would have been mentioned as a greater favor. Second, a hadith from Khalid ibn al-Walid that the Prophet forbade horse flesh. Additionally, horses are instruments of jihad, so eating them would diminish their availability. The dispute then shifts to whether the dislike is tanzihi or tahrimi. The text notes that the predominant view is tahrimi, but there is a report that Abu Hanifa retracted before his death, and the fatwa is on tanzihi. The final reconciliation: there is no real difference between the Imam and his companions because both agree on tanzihi dislike, even though the companions said it is halal with tanzihi. The ruling applies only to land horses; sea horses are unanimously not eaten.

Key Terms
الخيل (al-khayl): Horses, specifically land horses (al-khayl al-barr) as opposed to sea horses.
كراهة تنزيه (karahah tanzih): Disliked but not sinful; a level below makruh tahrimi.
صاحبيه (sahibayh): The two disciples: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on eating horse meat?
A) Halal without dislike
B) Makruh tahrimi
C) Makruh tanzih
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi on horse meat is makruh tanzih, a reconciled position between Abu Hanifa and his disciples.

Ruling on Eating Rabbit Meat
States the permissibility of rabbit meat with proof from hadith and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissibility of rabbit meat in Hanafi fiqh and identify the two proofs (hadith and qiyas) that establish it.

Core Idea
Rabbit meat is halal because the Prophet commanded his companions to eat it and it resembles the gazelle in not being a predator.

This short but essential ruling addresses the permissibility of rabbit meat. The Hanafi school relies on a clear prophetic command and a logical analogy to classify rabbit as halal. Understanding these proofs strengthens your ability to see how fiqh rulings are derived from both naql (text) and aql (reasoning).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? What is the hukm level (halal, haram, etc.)? The text states 'وحل الأرنب' – 'and the rabbit is permissible'.
The ruling is halal (permissible).
2
Locate the hadith proof
The author cites a hadith: when a roasted rabbit was presented to the Prophet, he ordered his companions to eat it. Identify the source (Ahmad and al-Nasa'i) and the key phrase 'أمر أصحابه أن يأكلوه' (he commanded his companions to eat it).
The command (amr) indicates permissibility in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy)
The second proof is a legal analogy: rabbit is not from the predatory animals (siba') and does not eat carrion, so it resembles the gazelle (zabi), which is known to be halal. Understand the illa (effective cause): lack of predatory nature and carrion-eating.
This analogy reinforces the ruling.
4
Confirm no counter-evidence
No conflicting evidence is mentioned, and the Hanafi school holds this as agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) within the madhhab.
Thus the ruling is clear and operative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحل الأرنب لأنه أمر أصحابه أن يأكلوه حين أهدي إليه مشويا رواه أحمد والنسائي؛ ولأنه ليس من السباع، ولا يأكل الجيف، فأشبه الظبي.
And the permissibility of rabbit (is established) because he (the Prophet) commanded his companions to eat it when it was presented to him roasted, narrated by Ahmad and al-Nasa'i; and because it is not among the beasts of prey, nor does it eat carrion, so it resembles the gazelle.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof regarding the permissibility of eating rabbit meat. The author provides two proofs: first, a hadith that when a roasted rabbit was presented to the Prophet, he instructed his companions to eat it. Second, a legal analogy (qiyas): the rabbit is not a predator (sabu') and does not eat carrion, making it similar to a gazelle (zabi), which is known to be halal. Thus rabbit is halal.

Key Terms
السباع (al-siba'): Predatory animals that hunt with fangs/claws.
الظبي (al-zabi): Gazelle, a halal animal known for being clean and not predatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof from hadith for the permissibility of rabbit meat?
A) The Prophet ate rabbit himself.
B) The Prophet commanded his companions to eat a roasted rabbit presented to him.
C) Rabbit is mentioned in the Quran as permissible.
D) The companions unanimously agreed on it without a hadith.

Key Takeaway
Rabbit is halal in Hanafi fiqh based on a prophetic command and analogy to the gazelle.

Halal Seafood: The Hanafi Restriction and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Restricts permissible sea creatures to fish that is not floating dead

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on permissible sea creatures, identify the proof-texts used by the opposing schools, and explain the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Only fish that is not floating dead (tafi) is halal; all other aquatic animals are prohibited.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute: are all sea creatures permissible to eat or only fish? The Hanafi school adopts a restrictive view, limiting halal seafood to fish that has not died floating on the surface. We will explore the proofs from both sides and the Hanafi response that maintains this position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: only fish that is not tafi (floating dead) is permissible from aquatic animals. All others – eels, crabs, shrimps, etc. – are not halal.
The term 'ma'iy' is defined as animals born and living in water, excluding land-born water-dwellers like some birds.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Malik, al-Shafi'i, and others hold that all sea creatures are halal, including predators, dogs, and even humans (with some Malikis excepting dogs and pigs).
This is a broad permissibility based on the generality of the Quranic verse.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opposing school cites Quran 5:96 'Permitted to you is the game of the sea' and the hadith 'Its water is pure and its dead is halal'. They also argue that sea creatures have no blood, so the prohibition of blood does not apply.
These proofs appear general and unconditional.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds by interpreting the verse on sea game as referring to hunting (not eating), and restricting the hadith on dead sea creatures to fish based on the hadith of 'two dead things' (fish and locusts). They also cite the verse on prohibiting impure things (khaba'ith) and specific prohibitions on frogs and crabs.
The Hanafi response uses textual restrictions and other proofs to narrow the permissibility.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis eat only fish from the sea (e.g., salmon, tuna) and avoid all other seafood (e.g., prawns, crabs, lobster). Followers of other madhhabs generally consider all seafood halal.
This difference affects daily dietary choices for millions of Muslims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يؤكل مائي إلا السمك غير طاف، ولا يؤكل مائي إلخ : المراد بالمائي مائي المولد والمعاش دون بري المولد مائي المعاش كبعض الطيور فإنه يؤكل، وقال مالك والشافعي وجماعة منهم عبد الرحمن ابن أبي ليلى يؤكل جميع حيوان البحر حتى الجريث والسباع والكلب والإنسان، واستثنى بعض المالكية الكلب والخنزير لهم قوله تعالى: ﴿أُحِلَّ لَكُمْ صَيْدُ الْبَحْرِ﴾ (المائدة: ٩٦) غير فصل، وقوله عليه في البحر: "هو الطهور ماؤه، والحل ميتته"، ولأنه لا دم في هذه الأشياء؛ لأن الدموي لا يسكن الماء، والمحرم هو الدم، فأشبه السمك. وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَيُحَرِّمُ عَلَيْهِمُ الْخَبَائِثَ ﴾ (الأعراف: (١٥٧ وما سوى السمك خبيث، ونهى النبي عليه عن التداوي بدواء اتخذ فيه الضفدع، وعن بيع السرطان والصيد في الآية محمول على الاصطياد، وهو مباح فيما لا يحل أكله، والميتة المذكورة في الحديث محمولة على السمك، وهو مستثنى؛ لقوله عليه: "أحلت الميتتان ودمان، أما الميتتان : فالسمك ،والجراد وأما الدمان فالكبد والطحال، والنصوص على تحريم السباع والخنزير مطلقة، فتتناول البري والبحري. واعلم أنه قال في "ملا" مسكين" وغيره: إن الخلاف في البيع والأكل واحد، وقال الزيلعي: ينبغي أن يجوز بيعه بالإجماع لطهارته. (ملخصا)
And no aquatic (animal) is eaten except fish that is not floating (tafi). The meaning of aquatic is that which is born and lives in water, not land-born but water-living like some birds, for these are eaten. And Malik, al-Shafi'i, and a group including Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla said: All sea animals are eaten, even eels, predators, dogs, and humans; some Malikis excepted dog and pig. Their proof is the verse: 'Permitted to you is the game of the sea' (al-Ma'ida 96) without distinction, and the hadith: 'Its water is pure and its dead is halal.' And because there is no blood in these things; since blood does not dwell in water, and what is prohibited is blood, so they resemble fish. And the verse: 'And prohibits for them the impure (khaba'ith)' (al-A'raf 157), and whatever is other than fish is impure. And the Prophet forbade treating with a frog potion, and forbade selling crab. And the 'game' in the verse is taken to mean hunting, which is permissible in what is not halal to eat. And the 'dead' mentioned in the hadith is taken to refer to fish, which is excused due to the hadith: 'Two dead things and two bloods are permitted: the two dead are fish and locusts, the two bloods are liver and spleen.' And the texts on prohibition of predators and pig are general, so they include land and sea. And know that it was said in 'Mulla Miskin' and others: The difference in selling and eating is the same. And al-Zayla'i said: It should be permissible to sell it by consensus because it is pure. (Summarized)
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding which sea creatures are halal to eat. The Hanafi position is that only fish that is not dead floating (tafi) is halal from the sea; other aquatic animals like eels, crabs, etc., are not. The opposing view, held by Malik, al-Shafi'i, and others, is that all sea creatures are halal based on the Quranic verse 'Permitted to you is the game of the sea' (al-Ma'ida 96) and the hadith 'Its water is pure and its dead is halal.' They argue that sea animals have no blood, so the prohibition of blood does not apply. The Hanafi response uses the verse 'He prohibits for them the impure' (al-A'raf 157) to argue that other than fish is impure. They also cite prohibitions on using frogs and selling crabs. The verse about sea game is understood to refer to hunting (which is permissible for non-edible animals), not eating. The hadith about dead sea creatures is restricted to fish, as the hadith of 'two dead things' specifically mentions fish and locusts. General prohibitions of predators and pig include both land and sea versions. The text notes that some, like al-Zayla'i, say it is permissible to sell such animals because they are pure, while others say the ruling on selling follows the ruling on eating.

Key Terms
مائي (ma'iy): An aquatic animal; born and living in water.
طاف (tafi): Dead fish floating on the water surface, died without obvious cause.
صيد البحر (sayd al-bahr): Game of the sea - used in Quran 5:96.
الخبائث (al-khaba'ith): Impure things – used in Quran 7:157.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which sea creatures are halal to eat?
A) All sea creatures
B) Only fish that is not dead floating
C) Only fish and shrimp
D) Only fish and crabs

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits only fish (not tafi) from the sea, based on the hadith of two dead things and the verse on khaba'ith, while other schools permit all sea creatures based on the general verse on sea game.

Ruling on Fish that Died Floating (Tafi)
Distinguishes permissible from impermissible dead fish based on cause of death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tafi fish and apply the principle of known cause of death to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
Tafi fish is haram unless the cause of death is known — mere floating does not establish permissibility.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on fish that die naturally and float to the surface (tafi). The school distinguishes between fish whose cause of death is known and those whose death is unknown. We will explore the evidence from hadith, the definition of tafi, and the principle that knowledge of the cause renders the fish halal. This ruling is contrasted with the views of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i, who permit tafi based on the general permissibility of sea creatures.

Guided Path
1
Define tafi
Read the Arabic definition: tafi is a fish that dies naturally (hataf anfihi) and floats with its belly up. Note that if the back is above, it is not tafi.
The position of the back is key to the definition.
2
Identify the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi school holds that tafi is not to be eaten; non-tafi fish (including those with known cause) are permissible.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Identify the opposing school's view
Malik and Shafi'i permit tafi based on the general permissibility of dead sea creatures.
The Hanafi response distinguishes between sea-cast and naturally dead fish.
4
Examine the Hanafi proof
The hadith states: 'What the water throws up, eat; what floats, do not eat.' This explicitly prohibits tafi.
The hadith is ahad and zanni in both transmission and meaning.
5
Apply the principle of known cause
If the cause of death is known (e.g., cast out by sea, trapped, killed by a bird, frozen, or cut), the fish is halal. If the cause is unknown (natural death in water), it is tafi and haram.
Even a tafi fish becomes halal if a known cause is detected, e.g., a visible wound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير طاف إلخ: [من طفى يطفو طفوا إذا علا هو الذي مات في الماء حتف أنفه فيعلوه منقلبا على ظهره] أي يؤكل السمك حال كونه غير طاف، وأما الطافي فلا يؤكل، وقال مالك والشافعي: لا بأس بأكل السمك الطافي؛ لأن ميتة البحر حلال للحديث، و قوله عليه: "ما تصب عنه الماء فكلوا وما طفا فلا تأكلوا"، ولا دليل لهما فيما روي؛ لأن المراد بميتة البحر: ما لفظه حتى تكون موته مضافا إلى البحر، ولا يتناول ما مات فيه بمرض أو نحوه، فالطافي: هو الذي مات في الماء حتف أنفه، فيعلو ويظهر منقلبا على ظهره، فإن كان ظهره من فوق، فليس بطاف، فيؤكل كما يؤكل ما في بطن الطافي. والأصل في هذا أن ما عرف سبب موته كلفظ البحر، أو بحبسه في مكان كالحظيرة الصغيرة بحيث يمكن أخذه غير حبلة، أو ابتلاع سمكة، أو قتل طير الماء إياها، أو إجماد الماء عليها حل أكلها؛ لأن سبب موتها معلوم، ولو مات . شدة الماء أو برده، أو انحسر الماء عن بعضه ومات روى هشام عن محمد: إن كان رأسه على الماء لا يؤكل، وإن كان ذنبه في الماء، ورأسه انحسر عنه الماء يؤكل؛ لأن خروج رأسه . الماء سبب لموته، فكان من معلوما بخلاف خروج ذنبه، فحاصله : أن الشرط فيه أن يعلم سبب موته حتى لو أبان عضوا بضرب فإنه يؤكل ويؤكل العضو. (ملتقطا من الشروح)
Non-floating (tafi) etc.: [From tafa yatfu tafwan when it rises; it is the one that died in water naturally and then rises turning over on its back.] Meaning fish is eaten while it is not tafi; as for the tafi, it is not eaten. Malik and al-Shafi'i said: There is nothing wrong with eating tafi fish because the dead of the sea is halal according to the hadith, and his statement: 'What the water throws up, eat; what floats, do not eat.' And there is no proof for them in what is narrated; because the intended meaning of 'dead of the sea' is what the sea casts out so that its death is attributed to the sea, and it does not include what died in it from illness or the like. So the tafi is the one that died in the water naturally, and it rises and appears turning over on its back. If its back is above, it is not tafi, so it is eaten just as what is in the belly of the tafi is eaten. The principle in this is that whatever has a known cause of death, such as being cast out by the sea, or being trapped in a small enclosure/fence so that it can be taken without a rope, or swallowing a fish, or a water bird killing it, or freezing of water upon it, then it is permissible to eat it, because the cause of its death is known. And if it dies due to strong current or cold, or the water recedes from part of it and it dies, Hisham narrated from Muhammad: If its head is in the water, it is not eaten; if its tail is in the water and its head is uncovered by water, it is eaten, because the exit of its head from water is a cause of its death, so it becomes known, unlike the exit of its tail. In summary: the condition is that the cause of its death be known, so that even if it cuts off a limb by a hit, it is eaten, and the limb is eaten. (Extracted from the commentaries)
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the term 'tafi' (floating dead fish) and its ruling. The Hanafi position is that only fish that is not tafi is permissible; tafi fish is not halal. The definition of tafi is a fish that dies in water naturally (hataf anfihi) and then floats to the surface with its belly up. If it floats with its back up, it is not considered tafi and is permissible. The opponent's view (Malik and Shafi'i) allows eating tafi based on the general permission of dead sea creatures. The Hanafi response distinguishes: the hadith about dead sea halal applies only to fish cast out by the sea; the authentic hadith says 'what the water throws up, eat; what floats, do not eat.' The text then lays out the principle: if the cause of death is known (e.g., thrown out by sea, trapped, eaten by a bird, frozen), the fish is halal. If the cause is unknown (natural death in water), it is tafi and haram. Examples are given from Hisham's narration from Muhammad: if the head is in water, it is haram; if the tail is in water but head exposed, it is halal because the cause is known. A final point: if a fish loses a limb due to a blow, both the fish and the limb are halal.

Key Terms
طاف (tafin): Fish that died naturally in water and floats with belly up.
حتف أنفه (hataf anfihi): Natural death without external cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a fish is found dead floating on water with its back above, what is the ruling?
A) It is tafi and not eaten
B) It is not tafi and is eaten
C) It is makruh
D) It is halal only if the fish is large

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires knowledge of the cause of death for a dead fish to be halal; a fish that dies naturally and floats with belly up (tafi) is haram.

Ruling on Locusts Without Slaughter
Ruling with proof and mention of ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on locusts, cite the hadith proof, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Maliki and Hanbali views.

Core Idea
Locusts are halal without any slaughter, even if found dead, based on the hadith of 'two dead things.'

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies that locusts are permissible to eat without any slaughtering process, even if they die naturally. The proof comes from the well-known hadith that permits two types of dead meat. We will examine the proof, the Hanafi position, and how it differs from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Locusts (al-jarad). What is the ruling? Halal without slaughter (bila dhaka).
Also note that it applies even if the locust dies naturally (mat hataf anfihi).
2
Locate the dalil
The passage references the hadith 'أحلت ميتتان ودمان' (two dead things and two bloods are permitted). Identify this as the proof.
This hadith is the basis for the permissibility of locusts and fish without slaughter.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The word 'mītatān' (two dead things) includes both fish and locusts. The Hanafi understanding is that these are unconditionally permissible even if found dead.
The passage notes a distinction: locusts are eaten even if dead naturally, unlike fish which require a known cause of death (tafi not halal).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text mentions that Imam Malik and Ahmad (in one narration) require a cause for the locust's death, e.g., breaking its head. Identify this as a cross-madhhab difference.
This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in ruling based on the method of death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحل بلا ذكاة كالجراد إلا أن الجراد يؤكل وإن مات حتف أنفه بخلاف السمك أي كما يحل الجراد بلا ذكاة، وعند مالك: لابد في موت الجراد من سبب وبه قال أحمد في رواية وعن مالك يعتبر قطف رأسه. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٦/٢] وما سبق من قوله عليه: "أحلت ميتتان إلخ لكن بينهما فرق، وهو أن الجراد يؤكل، وإن مات حتف أنفه بخلاف السمك. (عيني فتح)
And it is permissible without slaughter, like locusts, except that locusts are eaten even if they die naturally, unlike fish. That is, just as locusts are permissible without slaughter. According to Malik: a cause is required in the death of locusts, and Ahmad said in one narration, and from Malik: breaking its head is considered. And the previous hadith: 'Two dead things are permitted', but there is a difference: locusts are eaten even if they die naturally, unlike fish.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that locusts (jarad) are halal to eat without any slaughter, and even if they die naturally. This is unlike fish, which requires that the cause of death be known for permissibility. The hadith about 'two dead things being permitted' includes fish and locusts, but there is a distinction: fish that die naturally (tafi) is not halal, whereas locusts that die naturally are halal. The Maliki view requires a cause for the death of locusts, some even require the head to be cut off. The Hanafi school holds locusts as unconditionally halal based on the hadith and their natural type.

Key Terms
الجراد (al-jarad): Locusts; insects that are edible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how must locusts be killed to be permissible to eat?
A) By Islamic slaughter
B) By causing a specific injury like breaking the head
C) They are permissible even if they die naturally
D) They must be harvested alive and cooked

Key Takeaway
Locusts are halal without any slaughter or cause of death, based on the hadith and Hanafi understanding.

Signs of Life After Slaughter in Hanafi Fiqh
Determining halal meat through post-slaughter signs and intra-Hanafi differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for meat being halal based on post-slaughter signs, identify the specific signs of life and death, and apply the ruling to cases where the animal's life at slaughter is unknown.

Core Idea
Meat is halal if the animal was alive at slaughter – post-slaughter signs confirm life, but known life overrides any doubt.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh work addresses a practical problem: how to determine whether an animal was alive at the moment of slaughter when we are unsure. The scholars provide specific signs—movement and blood flow—but also discuss nuances and differences among Hanafi imams. Understanding these signs is crucial for distinguishing halal meat from carrion, especially in cases where the animal appears lifeless before slaughter.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Identify the two conditions that make meat halal when the animal's life at slaughter is unknown: movement or blood flow after slaughter. If neither occurs, the meat is not halal unless life is known.
The base ruling is from the matn: 'If it moved or blood came out, it is permissible, otherwise not, if its life is not known.'
2
Understand the rationale
The commentator explains that movement and blood flow only occur from a living animal; a dead animal does not move nor does blood flow actively. Therefore, their existence indicates life at the time of slaughter.
This is based on observable reality (aql) and supports the ruling.
3
Recognize the exception for known life
If the animal's life at slaughter is known with certainty (e.g., it was obviously alive before the cut), then the meat is halal even without any post-slaughter signs. This is due to the principle that certainty is not removed by doubt.
The Quranic proof is the exception clause in Surah al-Ma'idah 5:3.
4
Compare intra-Hanafi positions on blood flow
Abu Hanifa holds that blood flow indicates life only if it comes out as it does from a living animal (with force). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) consider any blood flow sufficient. Muhammad ibn Muqatil says blood flow without movement is not proof because blood may flow after death.
The author's wording favors the Sahibayn's view, but the stricter view is also transmitted.
5
Memorize the specific signs
For mouth: opening indicates death (relaxation), closing indicates life. For leg: drawing in indicates life, extending indicates death. For fur: standing up indicates life, lying flat indicates death. These signs are derived from the behavior of living versus dead animals.
These signs are used when the animal shows no general movement or blood flow, but subtle movements are present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الذبائح
ما يحل أكله وما لا يحل
وتحركت أو خرج الدم حل وإلا لا إن لم يدر حياته، وإن علم حل وإن لم يتحرك
أي الشاة
أي أكلها
لا يحل أكلها
حياته
وصلية
ولم يخرج الدم.
خرج الدم منها بعد الذبح من غير تحرك. حل إلخ أي حل أكلها في هذه الصورة؛ لأن الحركة وخروج الدم لا يكون إلا من الحي؛ لأن الميت لا يتحرك ولا يخرج منه الدم، فيكون وجودهما أو وجود أحدهما علامة الحياة فيحل، وعدمهما علامة الموت فلا يحل إلا إذا علم حياتها عند الذبح فتحل؛ لأن الأصل بقاء ما كان على ما كان، فلا يحكم بزوال الحياة بالشك، وذكر محمد بن مقاتل: إن خرج الدم ولم يتحرك، لا تحل؛ لأن الدم لا ينجمد عند موته، فيجوز خروج الدم بعد الموت. رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٧/٢] قال في "ملا مسكين" وفي "شرح الطحاوي": خروج الدم لا يدل على الحياة إلا إذا كان يخرج كما يخرج من الحي، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وهو ظاهر الرواية. قال في "الفتح": منه يعلم أن ما ذكره المصنف بقوله: "ولو ذبح شاة فتحركت أو خرج الدم حل" لا يتمشى على إطلاقه إلا على مذهب الصاحبين. حياته أي الشاة وقت الذبح وذكر الضمير على تأويل الغنم. وإن علم حل إلخ: يعني إن علم حياتها حل أكلها ولو لم تتحرك ولم يخرج منها الدم، كما لو ذبح المنخنقة أو الموقوذة أو المتردية أو النطيحة أو التي بقر الذئب بطنها وبها حياة حل مطلقا في ظاهر المذهب وإن كانت حياتها خفية؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِلَّا مَا ذَكَيْتُمْ﴾ (المائدة: ٣) من غير فصل، وعليه الفتوى، ولو ذبح شاة مريضة لا يعلم حياتها ولم يتحرك منها شيء إلا فمها، قال محمد سلمة: بن إن فتحت فاها لا تؤكل وإن ضمت توكل، وكذا في العيني: إن فتحت لا تؤكل وإن ضمت تؤكل، وفي الرجل إن قبضت رجلها تؤكل وإن مدت لا تؤكل، وفي الشعر إن نام شعرها لا تؤكل وإن قام تؤكل، وهذا لأن الحيوان يسترخي بالموت ففتح الفم والعين ومد الرجل، وقيام الشعر، ليس باسترخاء بل حركات تختص بالحي، فدلت على حياة المذبوح.
Book of Slaughtered Animals: What is permissible to eat and what is not. If it moved or blood came out, it is permissible, otherwise not, if its life is not known. And if its life is known, it is permissible even if it did not move. That is, the sheep. That is, eating it. Its eating is not permissible. Its life. And [؟] and no blood came out. Blood came out from it after slaughter without movement. It is permissible, etc., meaning its eating is permissible in this case; because movement and blood flow only occur from the living; because the dead does not move nor does blood come out of it. So their existence or the existence of one of them is a sign of life, so it is permissible. Their absence is a sign of death, so it is not permissible unless its life at the time of slaughter is known, then it is permissible; because the original rule is that what was remains as it was, and life is not judged to have ceased due to doubt. Muhammad ibn Muqatil mentioned: If blood came out and it did not move, it is not permissible; because blood does not coagulate at death, so blood may come out after death. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 2/367] He said in "Mulla Miskeen" and in "Sharh al-Tahawi": Blood flow does not indicate life unless it comes out as it comes out from the living. This according to Abu Hanifa, and it is the apparent narration. He said in "al-Fath": From this it is known that what the author mentioned in his statement: "If one slaughters a sheep and it moves or blood comes out, it is permissible" does not hold unrestricted except according to the view of the two companions. Its life, meaning the sheep at the time of slaughter, and the pronoun is mentioned based on interpretation of 'ghanam'. And if known, it is permissible, etc.: meaning if its life is known, its eating is permissible even if it did not move or blood came out, as in the case of slaughtering an animal that was strangled, beaten, fallen, gored, or had its belly torn open by a wolf, and it still has life—it is absolutely permissible in the apparent madhhab even if its life was faint; due to His statement: {Except what you slaughter} (al-Ma'idah 5:3) without separation, and fatwa is upon this. If one slaughters a sick sheep whose life is not known and nothing moves except its mouth, Muhammad ibn Salamah said: If it opens its mouth, it is not eaten; if it closes, it is eaten. Similarly in al-'Ayni: If it opens, it is not eaten; if it closes, it is eaten. As for its leg, if it draws its leg, it is eaten; if it extends, it is not eaten. As for its fur, if its fur lies flat, it is not eaten; if it stands up, it is eaten. This is because the animal relaxes at death, so opening the mouth, eye, and extending the leg (are signs of death), while standing of fur is not relaxation but movements specific to the living, thus indicating the life of the slaughtered animal.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling of when slaughtered meat is halal based on post-slaughter signs. The base ruling: if an animal moves or blood flows after slaughter, the meat is halal; if neither occurs and the animal's state before slaughter is unknown, it is not halal. The commentator explains that movement and blood flow are signs of life at the moment of slaughter, as the dead cannot produce these. If one of these signs occurs, it indicates life and thus halal. Absence of both indicates death before slaughter, making it carrion. However, if one knows for certain the animal was alive at slaughter, it is halal even without these signs, based on the principle that what is known to be alive remains so unless proven otherwise. The passage then examines a nuance: Muhammad ibn Muqatil held that if blood flows without movement, it is not proof of life, because blood may flow after death. But the relied-upon position, according to Abu Hanifa in the apparent narration, is that blood flow indicates life only if it comes out in the manner of living animals (with force). The text notes that the author's unqualified statement aligns with the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions). For animals killed by strangulation, beating, etc., if one knows they were alive when the throat was cut (even if life is faint), the meat is halal due to the Quranic exception. The passage ends with specific signs: opening the mouth or eye, extending the leg, or fur lying flat are signs of relaxation (death), while closing mouth/eye, drawing leg, or fur standing are signs of life. This is Hanafi method of distinguishing life from death by external signs.

Key Terms
ذكاة (tadhkiyah): Islamic method of slaughtering an animal by cutting the throat, which renders it halal if the animal was alive at the time.
علامة الحياة (alamat al-hayah): Physical signs indicating that an animal was alive at the moment of slaughter, specifically post-slaughter movement or blood flow in a manner characteristic of living animals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a sheep is slaughtered and only its mouth opens slightly, is the meat halal?
A) Yes, because any movement indicates life.
B) No, because opening the mouth is a sign of relaxation (death).
C) Yes, because the animal might have been alive.
D) No, because the animal must also kick or bleed.

Key Takeaway
When the animal's life at slaughter is unknown, post-slaughter movement or characteristic blood flow confirms life and renders meat halal; known life at slaughter makes post-slaughter signs unnecessary.
