Who Must Sacrifice? Conditions of Udhiyah
Defining udhiyah and listing conditions of obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for udhiyah to be wajib and identify who is exempt.

Core Idea
Udhiyah is wajib on the free, Muslim, resident, and wealthy; the poor, traveler, slave, and non-Muslim are exempt.

The Book of Sacrifice opens with a clear definition and conditions for obligation. Understanding these conditions is essential because the Hanafi school considers udhiyah wajib, and neglecting it when required is a sin. This passage also highlights a key intra-madhhab difference: Abu Hanifa holds it wajib, while the two companions consider it sunnah mu'akkadah. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is wajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The passage begins by stating that udhiyah is obligatory (تجب) under certain conditions. Recognize that the ruling is conditional.
The text says 'تجب الأضحية على حر مسلم مقيم موسر'.
2
Parse the four conditions
Separate each condition: حر (free), مسلم (Muslim), مقيم (resident), موسر (wealthy). Understand each one's opposite: slave, non-Muslim, traveler, poor – all exempt.
Traveler is defined as one on a journey of 48+ miles according to Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the exemption for minors
The default is that udhiyah is for oneself, not for one's young children. However, if the child has wealth, the guardian should sacrifice from that wealth in the relied-upon view.
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa that it is obligatory on the guardian for the child.
4
Note the ikhtilaf on obligation level
While Abu Hanifa says wajib, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say sunnah mu'akkadah. The text provides evidence for both: the desire-conditioned hadith for sunnah, and the severe threat for wajib. The mufta bihi is wajib.
The threat 'لا يقربن مصلانا' indicates a strong obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأضحية
أحكام الأضحية
كتاب الأضحية
على حر مسلم مقيم موسر عن نفسه لا عن طفله شاة أو سبع بدنة فجر يوم
فلا يجب على عبد
يسار الفطرة
هي من الإبل والبقر
كتاب الأضحية: [هي ما يضحى بها أي يذبح، وجمعها الأضاحي. ملا) (مسكين هي أفعولة وأصلها أضحوية، فاجتمعت الواو والياء، وسبقت إحداهما بالسكون فقلبت الواو ياء وأدغمت الياء في الياء، وكسرت الحاء لإثبات الياء، وتجمع على أضاحي بتشديد الياء كذا في "العناية"، قال في "المصباح" : يجوز في الأضحية ضم الهمزة في الأكثر وكسرها إتباعا لكسرة الحاء وجه المناسبة بين الكتابين: أن الأضحية من جنس الذبائح إلا أن الأولى أعم والثانية أخص، وإنما أفردها من الأول بكتاب على حدة؛ لأنها واجبة تثبت بشروط وأحكام وأسباب خاصة. واعلم أن وجوب الأضحية نسخ كل دم كان كان قبلها من العقيقة والعتيرة والعتيرة شاة كانت تذبح في رجب لله تعالى في ابتداء الإسلام. (من ملا مسكين، فتح) تجب: أي الأضحية وعن أبي يوسف أنها سنة وهو قول الشافعي وأحمد، وذكر الطحاوي أنها واجبة عند أبي حنيفة وسنة عندهما، وجه السنة: قوله عليها: "إذا رأيتم هلال ذي الحجة وأراد أحدكم أن يضحي، فليمسك عن شعره وأظفاره"، رواه مسلم ،وآخرون والتعليق بالإرادة ينافي الوجوب، ووجه الوجوب قوله عليه: "من وجد سعة فلم يضح فلا يقربن مصلانا"، رواه أحمد وابن ماجه، ومثل هذا الوعيد لا يلحق بغير الوجوب [رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٧/٢] على حر إلخ: يعني تجب الأضحية على . فلا تجب على العبد. "مسلم" فلا تجب على كافر. "مقيم موسر" سواء في مصر أو قرية فلا تجب على مسافر ؛ لقول عليه: "ليس على مسافر جمعة، ولا أضحية"، وعن مالك: لا يشترط الإقامة، ويستوي فيه المقيم بالمصر والقرى والبوادي. "موسر"؛ لأن العبادة لا تجب إلا على القادر، وهو الغني دون الفقير ومقداره ما يجب فيه صدقة الفطر رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٧/٢] عن نفسه إلخ: متعلق بقوله: "تجب"؛ لأنه أصل في الوجوب عليه وقوله : "لا" عن طفله" أي لا يجب عليه عن . أولاده الصغار في ظاهر الرواية، كما لا تجب عن عبده، وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة: يجب عليه أن عنه يضحي كصدقة الفطر. وتجب الأضحية في مال الصغير إذا كان له مال، ويأكل منه ما أمكنه، ويبتاع له بالباقي ما ينتفع بعينه، وقيل: يضحي عنها الأب أو الوصي . ماله عندهما، وعند محمد: من مال نفسه لا من مال الصغير، وبه قال زفر والشافعي، وقيل: لا يجوز التضحية من مال الصغير في قولهم جميعا، والأصح ما ذكرناه أولا. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٨/٢] شاة: مرفوع بـ "تجب" أي تجب شاة، أو بدل عن ضمير "تجب"، فيكون بيانا لمقدار الواجب. أو سبع بدنة: عطف على قوله: "شاة"، والقياس أن لا تجوز البدنة كلها إلا عن واحد؛ لأن الإراقة قربة واحدة، وهي لا تتجزأ، إلا أنا تركناه بأثر جابر الله، قال: نحرنا مع رسول الله البدنة عن سبعة. ولا نص في الشاة، فبقيت على أصل القياس، وتجوز عن ستة أو خمسة أو ثلاثة، ويشترط للإجزاء عن سبعة أن لا يكون لأحدهم أقل من سبع. (فتح)
طفله أي
Book of Sacrifice: Rulings of Sacrifice. Book of Sacrifice: Upon a free, Muslim, resident, wealthy person, for himself, not for his child, a sheep or a seventh of a camel/cow, at the time of Fajr of the day of (sacrifice). So it is not obligatory upon a slave. Capacity of fitrah. It is from camels and cows. Book of Sacrifice: It is that which is sacrificed, i.e., slaughtered; plural is adhahi. Mulla Miskeen: It is 'af'ulah, originally 'udhuhiyyah', then the waw and ya came together, one being preceded by sukun, so the waw is turned into ya and the ya assimilated, and the ha is broken to confirm the ya; it is pluralized as adhahi with tashdid of ya, as in al-'Inayah. He said in al-Misbah: It is permissible to say adhiyah with damm on the hamza in the majority, and kasr following the kasra of the ha. The connection between the two books (dhaba'ih and adhiyah) is that sacrifice is a type of slaughter, but the first is general and the second specific. He separated it into an independent book because it is obligatory and established with specific conditions, rulings, and causes. Know that the obligation of sacrifice abrogated every blood (sacrifice) that preceded it, including 'aqiqah and 'atirah, and the 'atirah was a sheep that was slaughtered in Rajab for Allah in early Islam. (From Mulla Miskeen, Fath) It becomes obligatory: i.e., the sacrifice. And from Abu Yusuf that it is sunnah, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad. Al-Tahawi mentioned that it is obligatory according to Abu Hanifa and sunnah according to them (the two companions). The evidence for sunnah: His statement (peace be upon him): "When you see the crescent of Dhul-Hijjah and one of you wants to sacrifice, let him refrain from his hair and nails" narrated by Muslim and others, and conditioning it on desire contradicts obligation. The evidence for obligation: His statement (peace be upon him): "Whoever has capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our place of prayer" narrated by Ahmad and Ibn Majah, and such a threat does not apply to other than obligated. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 2/367] "Upon a free" etc.: meaning the sacrifice is obligatory upon the free. So it is not obligatory upon a slave. "Muslim" so not obligatory upon a disbeliever. "Resident, wealthy" whether in a city or village, so not obligatory upon a traveler; due to his statement (peace be upon him): "There is no Friday prayer nor sacrifice upon a traveler." And from Malik: residence is not required, and it is equal for residents in city, villages, and deserts. "Wealthy" because worship is only obligatory upon the capable, and he is the rich, not the poor, and its measure is that which necessitates sadaqat al-fitr. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 2/367] "For himself" etc.: relates to His statement "it becomes obligatory" because it is the basis of obligation upon him. And his statement "not for his child" means it is not obligatory upon him for his young children in the apparent narration, just as it is not obligatory for his slave. And in the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa: it is obligatory upon him to sacrifice for them like sadaqat al-fitr. And the sacrifice is obligatory from the wealth of the minor if he has wealth; he (the guardian) eats from it as much as possible, and buys with the remainder what benefits him by its very use. And it is said: the father or guardian sacrifices for him from his wealth according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Muhammad: from his own wealth, not from the minor's wealth, and Zufar and al-Shafi'i also said this. And it is said: it is not permissible to sacrifice from the minor's wealth in all their sayings. And the most correct is what we first mentioned. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 2/368] "A sheep" is nominative by 'tajib' meaning a sheep is obligatory, or it is a substitute for the pronoun of 'tajib' making it an explanation of the obligatory amount. "Or a seventh of a camel/cow" is a conjunct to 'sheep'. The analogy is that a whole camel/cow is not permissible except for one person because the shedding of blood is a single act of devotion and cannot be divided, except we abandoned this due to the athar of Jabir (may Allah be pleased with him): he said: "We sacrificed with the Messenger of Allah a cow for seven and a camel for seven." And there is no text concerning the sheep, so it remains on the original analogy, and it is permissible for six, five, or three, and it is a condition for dividing into seven that none of them has less than a seventh. (Fath) His child, i.e.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Sacrifice (Udhiyah). It first provides the linguistic derivation: the word 'udhiyah' comes from the root meaning to sacrifice during the forenoon (duha). The commentator explains why this book is separate from the general book of slaughter: because udhiyah is a specific type of slaughter that is obligatory and has its own conditions and rulings. The passage then states the key conditions for whom udhiyah is obligatory: free (not a slave), Muslim (not a disbeliever), resident (muqim, not a traveler), and wealthy (mawsir, possessing the nisab of sadaqat al-fitr). The obligation is for oneself, not for one's young children (according to the apparent narration). There is a disagreement: Abu Hanifa considers it wajib, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is sunnah; the text cites evidence for both positions. The relied-upon view in the Hanafi school is that it is wajib. The passage then discusses the obligation regarding minors: if a child has wealth, the guardian must sacrifice from that wealth according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but Muhammad says from his own wealth, and the most correct view is that the guardian sacrifices from the child's wealth. Finally, it covers the number of participants in a camel or cow: up to seven persons can share, with each having at least a seventh share, based on the hadith of Jabir. Sheep is not shared; it is for one person only.

Key Terms
أضحية (udhiyah): The animal sacrificed on the days of Eid al-Adha (10th-12th Dhul-Hijjah) as an act of worship.
موسر (mawsir): A person who possesses the nisab of sadaqat al-fitr (roughly the equivalent of 200 dirhams or basic necessities surplus) at the time of sacrifice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on whom is udhiyah (sacrifice) obligatory?
A) Every adult Muslim, regardless of wealth or residence.
B) A free, Muslim, resident, and wealthy person – not on a traveler, slave, or poor person.
C) Only on the head of the household, including for his children.
D) On every Muslim who can afford it, including a slave.

Key Takeaway
Udhiyah is wajib on a free, Muslim, resident, wealthy person; others are exempt, and the school's mufta bihi is wajib despite the sunnah view of the two companions.

Obligation of Udhiya: The Wajib vs Sunnah Dispute
Defines udhiya and presents the wajib vs sunnah dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the legal status of udhiya, identify the evidence for both wajib and sunnah, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Udhiya is wajib according to Abu Hanifa due to a severe warning; it is sunnah according to the Sahibayn and other schools.

This lesson opens the Book of Udhiya (Sacrificial Animal). After a brief lexical definition, the author presents a fundamental dispute: is the sacrifice obligatory (wajib) or strongly recommended (sunnah mu'akkada)? This disagreement exists both within the Hanafi school and between schools. We will examine the evidence from hadith, the reasoning of each side, and the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position and its internal variant
Note that Abu Hanifa holds udhiya to be wajib, while his two main disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) consider it sunnah mu'akkada. This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf recorded in zahir al-riwayah.
Also note that Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad hold the sunnah view.
2
Examine the evidence for the sunnah position
The hadith: 'When you see the crescent of Dhu al-Hijja and one of you wants to sacrifice, let him refrain from his hair and nails.' The conditioning on 'wants' (arada) implies that the act is not obligatory; otherwise the prohibition would be unconditional.
This hadith is narrated by Muslim and others.
3
Examine the evidence for the wajib position
The hadith: 'Whoever has capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our place of prayer.' The severe threat of being barred from the mosque indicates that omitting the act is sinful, which is a characteristic of obligation (wajib).
Narrated by Ahmad and Ibn Majah.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the sunnah proof
The conditioning in the first hadith applies to the prohibition of cutting hair and nails, not to the sacrifice itself. The second hadith directly threatens those who omit sacrifice, thereby establishing its obligatory nature.
The commentator notes that such a severe warning does not attach to other than obligation.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) and the mufta bihi
If udhiya is wajib, leaving it without excuse is a sin and one must sacrifice on behalf of a deceased person who had the means. If sunnah, there is no sin, but the reward is great. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that udhiya is wajib, following Abu Hanifa.
Al-Tahawi confirms that the school's authoritative position is the wajib view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأضحية
كتاب الأضحية: هي ما يضحى بها أي يذبح، وجمعها الأضاحي. (مسكين) وهي أفعولة وأصلها أضحوية، فاجتمعت الواو والياء، وسبقت إحداهما بالسكون فقلبت الواو ياء وأدغمت الياء في الياء، وكسرت الحاء لإثبات الياء، وتجمع على أضاحي بتشديد الياء كذا في "العناية"، قال في "المصباح": يجوز في الأضحية ضم الهمزة في الأكثر وكسرها إتباعاً لكسرة الحاء. واعلم أن وجوب الأضحية نسخ كل دم كان قبلها من العقيقة والعتيرة والعتيرة شاة كانت تذبح في رجب لله تعالى في ابتداء الإسلام. (من ملا مسكين، فتح) تجب: أي الأضحية وعن أبي يوسف أنها سنة وهو قول الشافعي وأحمد، وذكر الطحاوي أنها واجبة عند أبي حنيفة وسنة عندهما، وجه السنة: قوله عليه: "إذا رأيتم هلال ذي الحجة وأراد أحدكم أن يضحي، فليمسك عن شعره وأظفاره"، رواه مسلم وآخرون والتعليق بالإرادة ينافي الوجوب، ووجه الوجوب قوله عليه: "من وجد سعة فلم يضح فلا يقربن مصلانا"، رواه أحمد وابن ماجه، ومثل هذا الوعيد لا يلحق بغير الوجوب.
Book of Sacrifice: It is what is sacrificed, i.e., slaughtered. Its plural is adahi. (Miskin) It is an af'ula pattern, originally adhawiyya, then the waw and ya came together, with one preceded by sukun, so the waw was changed to ya and the ya was assimilated, and the ha was broken to affirm the ya. It is pluralized as adahi with a shadda on the ya as in al-'Inaya. He said in al-Misbah: It is permissible to pronounce the hamza of al-udhiya with damm in most cases and with kasr following the kasra of the ha. Know that the obligation of udhiya abrogated every blood that was before it, such as aqiqa and atira. Atira was a sheep that was slaughtered in Rajab for Allah in early Islam. (From Mulla Miskin, Fath) It is obligatory: i.e., udhiya. And from Abu Yusuf that it is sunna, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad. Al-Tahawi mentioned that it is wajib according to Abu Hanifa and sunna according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The evidence for sunna: His statement, peace be upon him: "When you see the crescent of Dhu al-Hijja and one of you wants to sacrifice, let him refrain from his hair and nails," narrated by Muslim and others. The conditioning on will contradicts obligation. The evidence for obligation: His statement, peace be upon him: "Whoever has capacity and does not sacrifice, let him not approach our place of prayer," narrated by Ahmad and Ibn Majah. Such a severe warning does not attach to other than obligation.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage begins the Book of Udhiya (Sacrifice) with a lexical definition and then presents a core intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal status of udhiya. Abu Hanifa holds it to be wajib (obligatory), while his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), consider it sunnah mu'akkada (emphasized sunna). Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad agree with the Sahibayn. The text cites al-Tahawi, who confirms that the ruling is wajib according to Abu Hanifa and sunna according to the Sahibayn. The evidence for the sunna position is the hadith that conditions the prohibition of cutting hair and nails on the intention to sacrifice, which implies that sacrifice is not obligatory. The evidence for wajib is the hadith with a severe warning for those who have means but do not sacrifice, which indicates obligation. The commentator notes that such a severe warning is only attached to an obligation. The passage thus prepares the reader for a detailed treatment of the rules of udhiya.

Key Terms
أضحية (udhiya): The animal sacrificed on the days of Nahr, typically a sheep, goat, cow, or camel.
وجوب (wujub): Obligation; the ruling that an act is required, omission being sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Wajib
C) Fard
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that udhiya is wajib for those with financial capacity, based on the severe warning in the hadith.

The Animal for Udhiya: Sheep or Share of Large Animal
Specifies the minimum animal and presents the ruling on sharing large animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum animal required for udhiya in the Hanafi school and explain the ruling on sharing a large animal, citing the hadith of Jabir.

Core Idea
One sheep for one person, or one-seventh of a camel/cow per person — sharing is valid by explicit hadith.

In this lesson, we examine the type of animal required for udhiya (qurbani) according to the Hanafi school. The matn states that a sheep or a seventh of a camel (or cow) is obligatory. We will see how the school uses a hadith to override the analogy that would restrict a large animal to one person, and the condition for sharing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State what the matn says about the obligatory animal for udhiya.
The matn says 'a sheep or a seventh of a camel'.
2
Understand the analogy (qiyas)
Explain the qiyas that would restrict one large animal to one person.
Because slaughter is one indivisible worship.
3
Analyze the hadith of Jabir
Read the hadith and see how it overrides the qiyas.
The hadith explicitly shows sharing a cow/camel among seven.
4
Recognize the condition for sharing
Note the condition for sharing: each person must receive at least one-seventh.
5
Apply the ruling
Apply the ruling to a scenario, e.g., a group of five sharing a cow.
Five can share as long as each gets one-seventh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شاة: مرفوع بـ "تجب" أي تجب شاة، أو بدل عن ضمير "تجب"، فيكون بيانا لمقدار الواجب. أو سبع بدنة: عطف على قوله: "شاة"، والقياس أن لا تجوز البدنة كلها إلا عن واحد؛ لأن الإراقة قربة واحدة، وهي لا تتجزأ، إلا أنا تركناه بأثر جابر رضي الله عنه قال: نحرنا مع رسول الله الله البقرة عن سبعة، والبدنة عن سبعة. ولا نص في الشاة، فبقيت على أصل القياس، وتجوز عن ستة أو خمسة أو ثلاثة، ويشترط للإجزاء عن سبعة أن لا يكون لأحدهم أقل من سبع.
A sheep: it is raised by 'it is obligatory', i.e., a sheep is obligatory, or it is a substitute for the pronoun of 'it is obligatory', thus it is a clarification of the amount of the obligatory. Or a seventh of a camel: it is coordinated to his saying 'a sheep'. And analogy would require that the entire camel is not allowed except for one person, because the spilling of blood is one act of worship, and it is not divisible, except that we abandoned analogy by the hadith of Jabir, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: We sacrificed with the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, a cow for seven and a camel for seven. There is no text regarding the sheep, so it remains on the original analogy, and it is permissible for six, five, or three, and for sufficiency for seven it is a condition that no one has less than a seventh.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This lesson explains the type of animal required for udhiya. The matn specifies 'a sheep or a seventh of a camel' (or cow). The commentary elaborates: the word 'sheep' is the object of 'obligatory', meaning that a single sheep suffices for one person. For larger animals like camels and cows, analogy would require each animal to be for one person only, since the act of slaughter is a single worship. However, the hadith of Jabir (رضي الله عنه) allows up to seven persons to share one camel or cow. There is no text regarding the sheep, so it remains on the original analogy: one sheep per person. It is also permissible for fewer than seven to share a larger animal, e.g., six, five, or three, provided each receives at least one-seventh of the meat. This ruling is specific to Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
بدنة (badana): A camel (or cow) used for sacrifice; here it refers to a large animal that can be shared by up to seven people.
شاة (shah): A sheep or goat that suffices for one person's udhiya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Two
C) Seven
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
For udhiya, one sheep suffices per person, or up to seven can share a camel/cow provided each gets at least one-seventh.

Conditions for Udhiya: Who Must Sacrifice
Specifies conditions for obligation of udhiya and rulings for children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for udhiya to be wajib and explain the Hanafi ruling regarding sacrifice for a minor child.

Core Idea
Udhiya is wajib on the free, Muslim, resident, wealthy — not on a traveler or for a father's minor child.

This lesson presents the conditions that make the sacrifice of Udhiya obligatory. The matn lists four essential qualities: free, Muslim, resident, and wealthy. We will also examine whether a father must sacrifice for his young children, and what the school says about sacrificing from a minor's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is udhiya. The matn states it is wajib upon a person with four conditions: free, Muslim, resident, wealthy. State these conditions and note that the obligation is on the individual for himself, not for his minor child.
The ruling is wajib, not fard, due to evidence being zanni al-thubut.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'There is no Friday prayer and no sacrifice upon a traveler' (narrated by various compilers). This is the proof that residence is a condition.
The hadith is ahad, zanni al-thubut, but sufficient for establishing wajib.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit negation of udhiya for the traveler indicates that residence is a requirement. The absence of any similar exemption for the other conditions implies they must be present.
This is a straightforward dalalat al-nass (indication of the text).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik does not require residence. Within the Hanafi school, there is dispute over whether a father must sacrifice for his minor children: the zahir al-riwayah says no, but a riwaya from Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa says yes.
Also, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on whether to sacrifice from the child's property; the mufta bihi is to avoid it.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The most correct (asahh) is the first mentioned: not wajib on the father, and using the child's property is permissible but not preferred. The mufta bihi follows the zahir al-riwayah.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah is the relied-upon corpus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على حر مسلم مقيم موسر عن نفسه لا عن طفله شاة أو سبع بدنة فجر يوم
على حر إلخ: يعني تجب الأضحية على . فلا تجب على العبد. "مسلم" فلا تجب على كافر. "مقيم موسر" سواء في مصر أو قرية فلا تجب على مسافر ؛ لقول عليه: "ليس على مسافر جمعة، ولا أضحية"، وعن مالك: لا يشترط الإقامة، ويستوي فيه المقيم بالمصر والقرى والبوادي. "موسر"؛ لأن العبادة لا تجب إلا على القادر، وهو الغني دون الفقير ومقداره ما يجب فيه صدقة الفطر. رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٧/٢] عن نفسه إلخ: متعلق بقوله: "تجب"؛ لأنه أصل في الوجوب عليه وقوله : "لا" عن طفله" أي لا يجب عليه عن . أولاده الصغار في ظاهر الرواية، كما لا تجب عن عبده، وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة: يجب عليه أن عنه يضحي كصدقة الفطر. وتجب الأضحية في مال الصغير إذا كان له مال، ويأكل منه ما أمكنه، ويبتاع له بالباقي ما ينتفع بعينه، وقيل: يضحي عنها الأب أو الوصي . ماله عندهما، وعند محمد: من مال نفسه لا من مال الصغير، وبه قال زفر والشافعي، وقيل: لا يجوز التضحية من مال الصغير في قولهم جميعا، والأصح ما ذكرناه أولا.
On a free, Muslim, resident, wealthy person, for himself, not for his child: a sheep or a seventh of a camel, on the dawn day... "On a free" etc.: It means udhiya is obligatory on a free person, so it is not obligatory on a slave. "Muslim": so it is not obligatory on a disbeliever. "Resident, wealthy": whether in a city or village, so it is not obligatory on a traveler, because of his statement, peace be upon him: "There is no Friday prayer and no sacrifice upon a traveler." And from Malik: residence is not a condition, and it is equal for the resident of a city, village, and desert. "Wealthy": because worship is not obligatory except on the capable, and he is the rich, not the poor, and its measure is what necessitates sadaqat al-fitr. (Ramz al-Haqaiq) "For himself" etc.: It is related to his saying "it is obligatory" because it is the basis of obligation upon him. And his saying "not for his child" means it is not obligatory upon him for his young children in al-zahir al-riwaya, just as it is not obligatory for his slave. And in the riwaya of Hasan from Abu Hanifa: it is obligatory for him to sacrifice for them like sadaqat al-fitr. Udhiya is obligatory from the property of the minor if he has property, and he eats from it as much as possible, and with the rest he buys something of lasting benefit. And it is said: the father or guardian sacrifices from his (the minor's) property according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), but according to Muhammad: from his own property, not from the minor's, and Zufar and al-Shafi'i agreed with him. And it is said: it is not permissible to sacrifice from the minor's property according to all of them. The most correct is what we mentioned first.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This lesson presents the conditions for the obligation of udhiya. The matn states that udhiya is wajib upon a person who is free (not a slave), Muslim (not a disbeliever), a resident (not a traveler), and wealthy (having the nisab of sadaqat al-fitr). The commentary clarifies each condition with evidence, including the hadith that there is no sacrifice on a traveler. Regarding 'for himself, not for his child', the default rule is that a father is not obliged to sacrifice for his minor children. However, a riwaya from Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa states that it is obligatory, like sadaqat al-fitr. The text also addresses the case where the minor has wealth: the father or guardian may sacrifice from the minor's property according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but Muhammad (in another view) says it should be from his own money. The preferred view is that it is not permissible to use the minor's property for udhiya unless there is a specific need, but if done, it is considered a gift to the minor.

Key Terms
حر (hurr): Free person, not a slave. One of the conditions for obligation of udhiya.
مسلم (muslim): Believer in Islam. Udhiya is not obligatory on a non-Muslim.
مقيم (muqim): Resident, not a traveler. One of the conditions.
موسر (musir): Wealthy: possessing the nisab of sadaqat al-fitr (approximately 87 grams of gold or equivalent cash, though for udhiya it is defined as having surplus beyond basic needs).
صغير (saghir): Minor child who has not reached puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Free
B) Muslim
C) Resident
D) Married

Key Takeaway
Udhiya is wajib only on the free, Muslim, resident, wealthy adult; it is not obligatory on the father for his minor child, though permissible from the child's property with caution.

Acceptable Animals for Udhiya: Hornless, Castrated, and Crazy
Lists acceptable defects in sacrificial animals and their conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on animals with defects for udhiya and identify which defects are acceptable based on the Prophet's practice and the purpose of sacrifice.

Core Idea
Defects that do not impair meat quality do not invalidate udhiya.

When selecting an animal for udhiya, certain defects are overlooked because the purpose of sacrifice is the meat. This lesson examines the rulings on hornless, castrated, crazy, and mangy animals, clarifying that these are generally permissible, with some conditions. We will also see the proof from the Prophet's own practice for castrated animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animals listed
Read the matn: it mentions hornless (jamma'), castrated (khusi), and crazy (thawla'). The commentary adds mangy (jarba'). Note each defect.
The matn is concise; the commentary expands.
2
Analyze the ruling for hornless
The commentary states that horns serve no essential purpose, so a naturally hornless animal or one with a broken horn is acceptable unless the break reaches the skull.
The reasoning is based on the purpose of sacrifice.
3
Find the proof for castrated
The hadith is cited: the Prophet sacrificed two castrated rams. This establishes permissibility and preference. Abu Hanifa adds that castrated meat is tastier.
This is a clear dalil naqli.
4
Apply conditions to crazy and mangy
A crazy animal is acceptable only if it is fat and can still graze; a mangy animal is acceptable if fat and its skin is not damaged. These conditions ensure the meat is not affected.
The criterion is the quality of the meat.
5
Summarize the principle
The overarching principle: defects that do not compromise the meat, such as missing horns or castration, do not prevent sacrifice. Only defects that significantly harm the meat (like deep horn break or severe illness preventing grazing) make the animal unsuitable.
This aligns with the Hanafi leniency in such matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويضحي بالجماء والخصى والثولاء،
منزوع الخصيتين
هي المجنونة
ويضحي بالجماء إلخ: وهي التي لا قرن لها [خلقة]؛ لأن القرن لا يتعلق به مقصود، وكذا مكسورة القرن بل أولى؛ لما قلنا إلا إذا بلغ الكسر رأس العلظم. و الخصي"؛ لما صح أنه عليه ضحى بكبشين أملحين موجوئين. والأملح الذي فيه ملحة، وهو البياض الذي فيه شعيرات سود، وهو من لون الملح، والموجوء المخصي من الوجاء، وهو يضرب عروق الخصية بشيء، هكذا قاله الشارح قلت "المخصي" منزوع الخصيتين، والموجوء الذي تلوي عروق الخصية، فيصير كالخصي، وعن أبي حنيفة: أن الخصي أولى؛ لأن لحمه أطيب، "والثولاء" وهي المجنونة إن كانت سمينة، ولم يمنعها من السوم والرعي، وإن كان يمنعها منه لا يجوز، وكذا الجرباء إذا كانت سمينة، ولم يتلف جلدها جاز.
And one may sacrifice the hornless, the castrated, and the crazy. (Hornless) is one with no horns [naturally]; because no purpose is attached to the horn. Similarly, one with a broken horn is even more permissible, as we said, unless the break reaches the head bone. (Castrated): because it is established that he, peace be upon him, sacrificed two white-spotted, bruised wethers. Al-amlah is that which has whiteness, i.e., white with black hairs, and it is the color of salt. Al-mawju' is the castrated from al-waja', which is striking the veins of the testicle with something. This is what the commentator said. I say: al-mukhsii is one with testicles removed; al-mawju' is one whose testicle veins are twisted so he becomes like a castrated one. From Abu Hanifa: the castrated is better because its meat is tastier. (Al-thawla') which is the crazy one, if it is fat and it (the madness) does not prevent it from pasturing and grazing; but if it prevents it, it is not permissible. Similarly, the mangy (jarba') if it is fat and its skin is not damaged, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This lesson discusses the acceptability of animals with certain defects. The matn states that one may sacrifice a hornless (jamma'), castrated (khusi), and crazy (thawla') animal. The commentary explains: hornless is fine because no purpose is served by horns; similarly a broken horn is acceptable unless the break reaches the skull. Castrated is permissible because the Prophet himself sacrificed such animals, and Abu Hanifa said castrated is even better due to better meat. The crazy animal is acceptable if it is fat and the madness does not prevent it from grazing; otherwise it is not. The same applies to mangy animals: if fat and skin is intact, it is acceptable. These rulings are based on the principle that the purpose of sacrifice is meat; defects that do not affect the quality of meat are overlooked.

Key Terms
الجماء (al-jamma'): An animal born without horns; acceptable for udhiya.
الخصي (al-khusi): Castrated animal; its testicles are removed.
الموجوء (al-mawju'): An animal whose testicles are crushed but not removed; similar to castrated.
الثولاء (al-thawla'): A crazy animal.
الجرباء (al-jarba'): A mangy animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which animal is considered better for udhiya according to Abu Hanifa?
A) Hornless
B) Castrated
C) Crazy
D) Mangy

Key Takeaway
Minor defects that do not affect meat quality, such as being hornless or castrated, do not invalidate an animal for udhiya; only defects that significantly harm the meat or health make the animal unsuitable.

Mistaken Slaughter of Another's Udhiyyah: Istihsan Overrides Qiyas
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mistaken sacrifice validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi majority position on mistaken slaughter of another's sacrificial animal and evaluate Zufar's contrary view using the concepts of istihsan and qiyas.

Core Idea
Mistaken slaughter of another's sacrificial animal is valid, no liability, by istihsan.

When two people each mistakenly slaughter the other's sacrificial animal (udhiyyah), does the sacrifice count? Must they pay compensation? The Hanafi school's answer, based on istihsan (juristic preference), is that the sacrifice is valid and no payment is due. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and contrasts it with the strict qiyas position of Imam Zufar within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi majority position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani: the sacrifice is valid (sahih) and neither party is liable (daman).
The matn says 'صح عنهما، ولا يضمنان'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam Zufar (within the Hanafi school) disagrees. He holds the sacrifice is invalid and each must compensate the other.
Zufar's view is based on strict qiyas.
3
Follow the opponent's proof (qiyas)
Understand Zufar's analogy: the slaughterer is a transgressor (mutāddī) because he had no explicit permission, so he must guarantee the value, just as any usurper would.
Qiyas here treats the situation as unauthorized disposal of property.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response (istihsan)
See how the majority use istihsan to override qiyas. Because each animal was already designated (ta'yin) for sacrifice, the owner implicitly authorized the slaughter – the act is done by a proxy (wakīl), not a transgressor.
Istihsan finds a stronger reason (designation + need) to deviate from the analogy.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Identify the practical consequence: under Zufar's view, each pays the value of the other's animal; under the majority, no payment. The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is the majority, as the text states 'استحسانا'.
Tarjih is by the weight of the majority and istihsan's strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأضحية أحكام الأضحية ولو غلطا وذبح كل أضحية صاحبه، صح عنهما، ولا يضمنان. أي كل واحد منهما ذبحهما عن الأخرى خلافا لزفر استحسانا ولو غلطا إلخ أي الاثنان من أصحاب الأضاحي إذا غلطا وذبح كل واحد منهما أضحية صاحبه، صح الأضحية، ولا يضمنان ،استحسانا والقياس أن لا يجوز، ويضمن كل منهما لصاحبه، وهو قول زفر؛ لأنه متعد بالذبح بغير أمره فيضمن وجه الاستحسان: أنها تعينت للذبح؛ لتعيينها للأضحية، حتى وجب عليه أن يضحي بها بعينها في أيام النحر، ويكره أن يبدل بها غيرها، فصار المالك مستعينا بمن : يكون أهلا للذبح، فصار مأذونا له دلالة، ثم إذا جاز يأخذ كل واحد منهما أضحيته إن كانت باقية، ولا يضمنه؛ لأنه وكيله، فإن كان كل واحد أكل ما ذبحه يحلل كل واحد صاحبه فيجزئه؛ لأنه لو أطعمه الكل في الابتداء يجوز، وإن كان غنيا، فكذا يجوز له أن يحلله في الانتهاء، وإن تشاحا عن التحليل كان لكل واحد منهما أن يضمن صاحبه قيمة لحمه، ثم يتصدق بتلك القيمة؛ لأنها بدل عن اللحم ومن غصب شاة فضحى بها، ضمن قيمتها، وجاز عن أضحيته؛ لأنه ملكها بالغصب السابق، بخلاف ما لو كانت وديعة؛ لأنه يضمنها بالذبح، فلم يثبت له الملك إلا بعده. رمز الحقائق: ۳۷۱/۲
Book of Sacrifice, Rulings of Sacrifice: And if two people each mistakenly slaughter the other's sacrificial animal, it is valid for both, and they are not liable. That is, each of them slaughtered the other's. Contrary to Zufar, by istihsan. 'And if two people each mistakenly...' meaning the two owners of sacrificial animals: when they err and each slaughters the other's animal, the sacrifice is valid and they are not liable by istihsan. The analogy (qiyas) is that it is not permissible and each is liable to the other—this is Zufar's view, because he is a transgressor by slaughtering without permission, so he is liable. The istihsan basis: the animal had already been designated for slaughter, because it was specifically set apart for sacrifice, such that it becomes obligatory to sacrifice that very animal on the days of Nahr, and it is disliked to replace it. Thus the owner has taken assistance from someone qualified to slaughter, so permission is implied. Then if valid, each takes his own animal if it remains, and is not liable because he is his agent. If each ate what he slaughtered, each must make it lawful for the other (tahlil), and it suffices; because if he had fed him all of it initially it would be permissible even if the recipient was wealthy, so likewise it is permissible for him to make it lawful at the conclusion. If they dispute regarding tahlil, each may claim the value of the meat from the other, and then give that value in charity, as it is a substitute for the meat. And if someone usurps a sheep and sacrifices with it, he is liable for its value, but it suffices for his sacrifice, because he owned it through the prior usurpation – unlike the case of a deposit, where he is liable for the slaughter and ownership is established only after the act. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 371/2)
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding two people who each mistakenly slaughter the other's sacrificial animal. The matn states that the sacrifice is valid and neither is liable. The commentator explains that this is by istihsan (juristic preference), while qiyas (analogy) would require invalidity and liability, which is the view of Zufar. The istihsan reasoning is that each animal had already been specifically designated (ta'yin) for sacrifice, making it incumbent to slaughter that very animal. Therefore, the owner is deemed to have implicitly authorized the other's act, as he sought help from a qualified person. Consequently, the slaughterer is considered an agent. If the animals are still intact, each takes his own; if consumed, they must mutually make it lawful (tahlil) or else pay the value of the meat as charity. The commentary further distinguishes from a case of usurpation: if one usurps a sheep and sacrifices it, he owns it through usurpation (since ownership is established by taking a substitute), but if it were a deposit, he would not own it and would be liable for the value, as ownership only arises after the slaughter.

Key Terms
أضحية (udhiyyah): The sacrificial animal slaughtered on Eid al-Adha, also called Qurbani.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from qiyas for a stronger reason, used in this case to validate the sacrifice.
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay compensation for a property or for damages.
تعيين (ta'yin): Designation or earmarking of a specific animal for sacrifice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if two people each mistakenly slaughter the other's sacrificial animal, what is the ruling?
A) The sacrifice is invalid and each must compensate the other.
B) The sacrifice is valid and neither is liable, by istihsan.
C) Only the one who began the slaughter is liable.
D) It is valid but each must give half the meat in charity.

Key Takeaway
By istihsan, mistaken slaughter of another's sacrificial animal is valid and no liability arises, overriding the strict analogy.

Classification of Makruh Tahrimi: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition and legal weight of makruh tahrimi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the legal classification of makruh tahrimi and evaluate the evidentiary basis for the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Makruh tahrimi is closer to haram but not identical – a verbal dispute rooted in default permissibility versus caution.

Within the Hanafi school, there is a nuanced disagreement on how to label acts that are prohibited but lack definitive evidence (nass qat'i). The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary clarifies that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider makruh tahrimi as 'closer to haram', while Imam Muhammad equates it with haram. This difference is largely verbal, but understanding it sharpens your grasp of Hanafi legal epistemology and the role of evidence classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Find the statements: 'makruh is closer to haram' (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and 'every makruh is haram' (Imam Muhammad). Note that this applies to makruh tahrimi.
Use the pronouns: 'indahuma' = Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Muhammad's view is attributed separately.
2
Understand the evidentiary basis
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) rely on the default principle that things are permissible (ibaha asliyya) unless there is decisive evidence of prohibition. Muhammad, on the other hand, holds that without definitive proof of permissibility, caution dictates labeling it haram.
This mirrors the wajib/fard distinction: both are obligatory but fard has stronger evidence.
3
Distinguish makruh tahrimi from makruh tanzihi
The text clarifies that the dispute only concerns makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). Makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) is unanimously closer to halal.
Makruh tanzihi is not sinful; it is better to avoid.
4
Recognize the verbal nature of the dispute
The commentary states that the conflict may be lafzi (verbal). Both sides agree on the practical ruling – avoidance. The difference is in the label due to different thresholds of evidence.
Imam Muhammad's usage is analogous to calling wajib 'fard' in a broader sense.
5
Apply to practice
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: makruh tahrimi is not haram but is closer to it. A mufti will advise avoidance but not equate it with a sin established by definitive proof.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is minimal; both agree the act should be avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية المكروه إلى الحرام أقرب، ونص محمد أن كل مكروه حرام. عندهما المكروه إلخ: هذا عندهما، وهذا في المكروه التحريمي، وقال خلف بن يحي المكروه إلى الحلال أقرب، وأما التنزيهي: فإلى الحلال أقرب اتفاقا كما في 'فتح باب العناية'، وإنما كان إلى الحرام أقرب؛ لتعارض الأدلة فيه، وتغليب جانب الحرمة (فتح) حرام وإنما لم يطلق عليه لفظ الحرام؛ لأنه لم يجد فيه نصا قطعيا، فكان نسبة المكروه إلى الحرام عند محمد كنسبة الواجب إلى الفرض تكملة البحر الرائق: ۳۵۹/۸ قال في 'الفتح' نقلا 'فتح باب العناية': ولعل الخلاف فيه لفظي، وحاصله : أن محمدا أطلق عليه حراما ولم يجعله حلالا؛ لعدم قاطع بالحل، وهما جعلاه حلالا؛ لأنه الأصل في الأشياء، ولعدم قاطع بحرمته.
Book of Disliked Acts. The disliked (makruh) is closer to the forbidden (haram). And the explicit text of Muhammad is that every makruh is haram. According to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). 'The disliked, etc.': this is according to them, and this applies to makruh tahrimi. And Khalaf ibn Yahya said: the disliked is closer to the permissible. As for makruh tanzihi, it is closer to permissible by consensus, as in Fath Bab al-Inayah. It is closer to haram only because of conflicting evidences and favoring the side of prohibition (Fath). 'Haram' – but he did not absolutely call it haram because he did not find a definitive text (nass qat'i). So the relationship of makruh to haram according to Muhammad is like the relationship of wajib to fard. (Takmila al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/359). He said in al-Fath quoting Fath Bab al-Inayah: 'Perhaps the difference is verbal; the gist is that Muhammad called it haram and did not make it permissible because there is no definitive proof of permissibility, while they (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) made it permissible because permissibility is the original state of things, and there is no definitive proof of prohibition.'
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the definition of 'makruh' (disliked). The matn states that according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (referred to as 'them'), makruh is closer to haram, while Imam Muhammad says every makruh is haram. The commentator clarifies that this applies to makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked); makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) is closer to halal by consensus. The reason for the dispute is the strength of evidence: Muhammad equates makruh with haram due to the absence of a definitive proof of permissibility, whereas the two imams consider permissibility the default and require a definitive proof for prohibition. The commentator notes that the difference may be verbal (lafzi). A helpful analogy: Muhammad's classification is like the relationship between wajib and fard—both are obligatory but with different evidentiary strength.

Key Terms
مكروه تحريمي (makruh tahrimi): A prohibitively disliked act; closer to haram and sinful if committed, but without definitive evidence.
مكروه تنزيهي (makruh tanzihi): A lightly disliked act; not sinful but better to avoid.
عندهما (indahuma): According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), as opposed to Imam Muhammad.
نص قطعي (nass qat'i): A definitive text (Quran or mutawatir hadith) that is clear and unambiguous.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), how is makruh tahrimi related to haram?
A) It is exactly the same as haram.
B) It is closer to haram but not identical.
C) It is closer to halal.
D) It is equal to wajib.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes two levels of makruh; the dispute between Imam Muhammad and the other two imams over the proximity of makruh tahrimi to haram is largely verbal, and the relied-upon view retains a distinction between makruh tahrimi and haram.

Prohibition of Silk for Men and Concession for Small Amounts
Ruling on wearing silk: prohibited for men, permitted for women, excused for four fingers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on silk for men and women, identify the hadith proof, and apply the four-finger concession.

Core Idea
Silk is haram for men but halal for women; a small amount (four fingers) is excused.

This passage from the section on clothing (fī al-lubs) establishes the foundational ruling on wearing silk for men and women. While silk is generally prohibited for men, a narrow concession is granted for a small amount, defined as four fingers in width. The ruling is supported by explicit hadith evidence and is a well-known position in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that wearing silk (ḥarīr) is ḥarām for men and ḥalāl for women. The linguistic note: the lām in 'li al-rajul' means 'upon' (ʿalā), emphasizing that the prohibition applies to men.
This is the general rule; the exception follows.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith from Abū al-Ashʿarī Mūsā is cited: 'Gold and silk are permitted for the females of my ummah and prohibited for its males.' The ḥadīth is reported by Aḥmad, al-Nasāʾī, and al-Tirmidhī, who deemed it ṣaḥīḥ.
The word 'ḥarrim' (prohibited) indicates taḥrīm (prohibition) in Hanafi uṣūl.
3
Note the concession for a small amount
A second ḥadīth (reported by Aḥmad, Muslim, and al-Bukhārī) shows that the Prophet forbade silk except for the space of two, three, or four fingers. The Hanafi school takes the highest reported number—four fingers—as the pardoned amount.
This is the muftā bihī position: the maximum excused area is four fingers in width.
4
Understand the epistemological weight
Both ḥadīths are āḥād (single-transmitter) in thubūt, making them ẓannī al-thubūt, but their dalāla (indication) is qaṭʿī because the command/forbidding is explicit. The ruling is from Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah and firmly established.
The concession is not an override but a specific exception proven by another ḥadīth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في اللبس: قدم الأكل والشرب لشدة الحاجة إليه، ثم اللبس كذلك بالنسبة لما بعده. (فتح) حرم للرجل إلخ: يعني يحرم على الرجل لا على المرأة لبس الحرير، واللام تأتي بمعنى "على" قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنْ أَسَأْتُمْ فَلَهَا﴾ (الإسراء: (۷) أي فعليها، وإنما حرم لبس الحرير على الرجال دون النساء؛ لما روى أبو الأشعري موسی أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: "أحل الذهب والحرير للإناث من أمتي وحرم على ذكورها"، رواه أحمد والنسائي والترمذي، وصححه إلا أن اليسير معفو عنه، وهو مقدار أربع أصابع؛ لما روى أحمد ومسلم والبخاري: "نهى عن لبس الحرير إلا موضع أصبعين أو ثلاثة أو أربع الحديث تكملة البحر الرائق: ۳۷۸/۸]
Section on clothing: He prioritized eating and drinking due to stronger need, then clothing similarly relative to what follows. (Fath) 'Prohibited for men, etc.' means it is prohibited for men and not for women to wear silk. The lam comes in the meaning of 'ala (upon). Allah said: 'And if you do evil, it is upon them' (al-Isra 17:7), i.e., upon them. The prohibition of silk for men and not for women is based on the hadith of Abu al-Ash'ari Musa that the Prophet said: 'Gold and silk are permitted for the females of my ummah and prohibited for its males.' Reported by Ahmad, Nasa'i, Tirmidhi, who declared it sahih. However, a small amount is pardoned, which is the extent of four fingers, based on the hadith reported by Ahmad, Muslim, and Bukhari: 'He forbade wearing silk except for the space of two, three, or four fingers.' (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8/378)
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This section begins the discussion of clothing. The author explains the prohibition of silk for men, which is haram, but it is halal for women. The linguistic point: the lam in 'li al-rajul' here means 'upon' (ila). The proof is a sahih hadith. However, a small amount is excused (ma'fu 'anhu), defined as four fingers' width. This concession comes from another hadith. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school. The hadiths are clear on both the principle and the exception.

Key Terms
حرير (ḥarīr): Silk cloth; its wearing is prohibited for men.
عفو (ʿafw): Pardon/excusing; the small amount (four fingers) is not sinful.
يسير (yasīr): Small amount; the pardoned quantity of silk for men.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Prohibition of Silk for Men and the Four-Finger Exception
Ruling with proof: prohibition and exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the primary ruling on silk for men and women, cite the hadith proofs, and explain the exception of four fingers.

Core Idea
Silk is haram for men but halal for women; a small amount (four fingers) is pardoned.

This lesson establishes the foundational ruling on silk. The passage first clarifies that silk is forbidden for men but permissible for women, based on a hadith. It then introduces the exception of a small amount, measured as four fingers, and discusses the precise measurement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and what the hukm is. The passage states: 'حرم للرجل... لبس الحرير' – wearing silk is haram for men.
The ruling is clearly about wearing, not other uses.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith that forms the basis of the prohibition. The passage cites the hadith of Abu al-Ash'ari Musa: 'أحل الذهب والحرير للإناث من أمتي وحرم على ذكورها'.
This hadith makes the ruling explicit.
3
Analyze the exception
The passage then mentions an exception: 'اليسير معفو عنه، وهو مقدار أربع أصابع'. Understand that a small amount (four fingers) is pardoned.
The second hadith shows the prohibition is not absolute.
4
Examine the measurement of four fingers
The passage discusses how the four fingers should be measured: whether spread or in natural state, with preferred opinion being spread (al-Karabisi).
This clarifies the practical application.
5
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: silk is haram for men, but a small amount (four fingers) is allowable. The exact measurement is the spread of four fingers.
This is the standard Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في اللبس: قدم الأكل والشرب لشدة الحاجة إليه، ثم اللبس كذلك بالنسبة لما بعده. (فتح) حرم للرجل إلخ: يعني يحرم على الرجل لا على المرأة لبس الحرير، واللام تأتي بمعنى "على" قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنْ أَسَأْتُمْ فَلَهَا﴾ (الإسراء: (۷) أي فعليها، وإنما حرم لبس الحرير على الرجال دون النساء؛ لما روى أبو الأشعري موسی أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: "أحل الذهب والحرير للإناث من أمتي وحرم على ذكورها"، رواه أحمد والنسائي والترمذي، وصححه إلا أن اليسير معفو عنه، وهو مقدار أربع أصابع؛ لما روى أحمد ومسلم والبخاري: "نهى عن لبس الحرير إلا موضع أصبعين أو ثلاثة أو أربع الحديث تكملة البحر الرائق: ۳۷۸/۸] 11  ما يتعلق باللبس كتاب الكراهية ٣٣٣ لبس الحرير إلا قدر أربعة أصابع وحل توسده وافتراشه ولبس ما سداه حرير ولحمته قطن أو خز، وعكسه حل في الحرب فقط، لحمة الثوب ما ينسج عرضا الديباج : مس لا في غيره لبس الحرير ولو بحائل بينه وبين بدنه على الصحيح وفي الحرب أيضا عنده وقالا: يحل في الحرب وكذا مطلقا، سواء كان في الحرب أو غيره، وسواء كان بحائل أو بدونه، وروي عن الإمام إنما يحرم إذا الجلد، قال في "القنية": وهي رخصة عظيمة في موضع عمت به البلوى وقالا لا بأس بلبس الديباج والحرير في الحرب، ويحل لبس الحرير لدفع قمل أو حكة كما في "الأشباه"، والفرق بين الحرير والديباج: أن المراد بالحرير ما سداه حرير ولحمته قطن والمراد بالديباج ما سداه ولحمته حرير. فتح بالتقاط من مواضع) أربعة أصابع: قال في "الفتح": وفي "شرح المقدسي": المراد بالأصابع أصابع انه لا مضمومة كل الضم، ولا منشورة كل النشر ، أي على هيئتها كما هي وقال الكرماني منشورة، قال الكرابيسي: وهذا أولى. انتهى قال في "الدر المختار" نقلا عن "القنية": عمامة طرزها قدر أربع أصابع من إبر يسم من أصابع . قيس شبرنا يرخص فيه، وقال في رد المحتار" عند هذا القول: وقد رمز فيها أي في "القنية" نجم الأئمة: المعتبر علي هيئتها لا أصابع السلف، ثم رمز للكرماني منشورة، ثم رمز للكرابيسي التحرز عن أربع أصابع كما هي مقدار المنشورة أولى، فاحفظه. وحل توسده إلخ أي توسد الحرير وافتراشه عند أبي حنيفة وقالا يكره؛ لما روي عن حذيفة ه: "أنه عليه نهانا أن نشرب في آنية الذهب والفضة، وأن نأكل فيها، وعن لبس الحرير والديباج، وأن نجلس عليه"، رواه البخاري، و به قالت الثلاثة وذكر الخلاف هكذا في "القدوري" وصاحب "المنظومة" و"المجمع"، وذكر في "الجامع الصغير" الخلاف بين أبي حنيفة ومحمد وذكر أبو الليث أن أبا يوسف مع أبي حنيفة، ولهم ما روي أن النبي على مرفقة حرير؛ ولأن الحرام هو اللبس والافتراش ليس بلبس وكذا يباح النوم عليه عنده، وجعله سترا وتعليقه على الباب خلافا لهم، وفي نوادر هشام أن محمدا قال: أكره تكة الديباج والإبريسم، وفي "الفتاوى الصغرى: ولا بأس بتكة الحرير عند أبي حنيفة رمز الحقائق: ٣٧٥/٢] ولبس ما سداه إلخ: [هو ما يمد طولا في النسج لأن الصحابة لها لبسوا مثل هذا، ولأن الثوب لا يصير ثوبا إلا بالنسج، والنسج باللحمة، فكانت المعتبرة، أو نقول : لا يكون ثوبا إلا بهما، فتكون العلة ذات فيعتبر آخرهما، وهو اللحمة؛ ولأن اللحمة . التي تظهر في النظر ، فتكون العبرة لما يظهر دون ما يخفى. واعلم هي هي أنه يحل لبس ما سداه حرير ولحمته خز في الحرب وغيره بالإجماع. (فتح عيني) أو خز: هو اسم دابة من البحر ثم سمي الثوب المتخذ من وبره خزا. ملا مسكين حل في الحرب إلخ: يعني عكس وهو أن يكون لحمته حريرا وسداه قطنا أو خزا أو غيرهما، لا يجوز إلا في الحرب؛ لما ذكرنا أن العبرة باللحمة تكملة البحر الرائق: ۳۸۰/۸] وهذا أيضا بالإجماع للضرورة؛ لأنه أهيب وأدفع لمعرة السلاح = المذكور،
Section on clothing: He prioritized eating and drinking due to severe need, then clothing similarly relative to what follows. (Fath) 'Forbidden for the man etc.' means: It is forbidden for a man, not for a woman, to wear silk, and the lam comes in the meaning of 'upon' as Allah said: {And if you do evil, it is upon them} (al-Isra 7) i.e., upon them. Silk is forbidden for men but not women due to what Abu al-Ash'ari Musa narrated: the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Gold and silk are made lawful for the females of my ummah and forbidden for its males.' Narrated by Ahmad, Nasa'i, Tirmidhi, and he authenticated it, except that a small amount is pardoned, and it is the extent of four fingers. For Ahmad, Muslim, and Bukhari narrated: 'He forbade wearing silk except for the place of two or three or four fingers' (hadith). Takmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/378. [Page 334] What pertains to clothing: Book of Karahiyah 333. Wearing silk except for the amount of four fingers. And it is permissible to use it as a pillow and to sit on it, and to wear what has silk warp and cotton or khazz weft; and its opposite is permissible only in war. The weft of a garment is what is woven widthwise. Dibaj: touch (?) not in other than it. Wearing silk even with a barrier between it and his body is the correct view, and in war also according to him (Abu Hanifa). And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is permissible in war, and likewise absolutely, whether in war or otherwise, whether with a barrier or without. And it is narrated from the Imam that it is only forbidden if (touches) the skin. He said in al-Qunyah: It is a great concession in a place of widespread need. And they (the two) said: There is no harm in wearing dibaj and silk in war. And wearing silk is permissible to repel lice or itching, as in al-Ashbah. The difference between silk (harir) and dibaj is that harir refers to what has silk warp and cotton weft, while dibaj refers to what has silk warp and silk weft. (Fath, collected from places). Four fingers: He said in al-Fath: In Sharh al-Maqdisi: What is meant by fingers is fingers that are neither completely clasped nor completely spread, i.e., in their natural state. Al-Kirmani said: Spread out. Al-Karabisi said: This is preferable. End. He said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar quoting al-Qunyah: A turban embroidered with the amount of four fingers of needles measuring from fingers? measuring our span is permitted. And in Radd al-Muhtar: He alluded in it (al-Qunyah) by Najm al-A'imma: What is considered is the state of the fingers, not the fingers of the Salaf. Then he alluded to al-Kirmani as spread, then to al-Karabisi for caution about four fingers as they are - the amount of spread is better, so remember it. 'And it is permissible to use it as a pillow etc.' means using silk as a pillow and sitting on it (tawassud and iftirash) is permissible according to Abu Hanifa, but they (the two) say it is disliked. For it is narrated from Hudhayfah (may Allah be pleased with him) that 'He (the Prophet) forbade us from drinking in vessels of gold and silver, and eating in them, and from wearing silk and dibaj, and from sitting on it.' Narrated by Bukhari, and the three (imams) held that. The disagreement is mentioned thus in al-Quduri, the author of al-Manzumah, and al-Majma'. And it is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir as a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu al-Layth mentioned that Abu Yusuf is with Abu Hanifa. And they (the two) have what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) (used) a silk cushion, and because the forbidden is wearing, and using as a pillow is not wearing. Similarly, sleeping on it is permissible according to him, and making it a curtain or hanging it on the door is a disagreement with them. In Nawadir Hisham, Muhammad said: I dislike a waistband (tikka) of dibaj and ibrisam (silk thread). And in al-Fatawa al-Sughra: There is no harm in a silk waistband according to Abu Hanifa. (Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/375) 'And wearing what has silk etc.' means what is stretched lengthwise in weaving, because the Companions wore such, and because a garment only becomes a garment through weaving, and weaving is by the weft, so it is considered; or we say: it does not become a garment except by both, so the effective cause is shared and the last of them is considered, which is the weft; and because the weft is what appears in sight, so consideration is given to what appears, not what is hidden. Know that it is permissible to wear what has silk warp and khazz weft in war and otherwise by consensus. (Fath, Ayni) 'Or khazz' is the name of a sea animal, then the garment made from its fur is called khazz. (Mulla Miskin) 'Its opposite is permissible only in war' means its opposite, i.e., when the weft is silk and the warp is cotton or khazz or other, it is not permissible except in war, because we mentioned that the consideration is given to the weft. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/380) And this is also by consensus due to necessity, because it is more awe-inspiring and repels the harm of weapons = as mentioned.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the rulings of silk (harir) and related fabrics for men. It begins by stating that silk is haram for men but halal for women, based on a hadith. However, a small amount (four fingers' width) is pardoned. The exact measure of 'four fingers' is discussed: whether they are spread or natural. The passage then addresses several related issues: (1) Sitting on silk (tawassud and iftirash): Abu Hanifa permits it, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider it makruh. The former relies on a report that the Prophet used a silk cushion, and that the prohibition is specifically for wearing. (2) Wearing silk in war: Abu Hanifa forbids it even with a barrier, but the Sahibayn permit it in war as a concession, citing the need for a formidable appearance. (3) Fabrics with mixed fibers: If the warp is silk and the weft is cotton or khazz, wearing is permissible by consensus (as the weft is considered). If the opposite (silk weft, cotton warp), it is only permissible in war. (4) The definition of dibaj: It is pure silk (both warp and weft), whereas harir in this context refers to mixed fabric with silk warp and cotton weft. (5) Silk waistbands (tikka): There is a difference between Abu Hanifa (permissible) and Muhammad (disliked). The passage ends by reinforcing that the weft determines the ruling, and that wearing such mixed fabrics in war is permissible by consensus for necessity.

Key Terms
حرير (ḥarīr): Silk fabric; in this context, often refers specifically to fabric with silk warp and cotton weft (mixed silk).
ديباج (dībāj): Pure silk fabric where both warp and weft are silk.
اربعة اصابع (arba'a aṣābi'): Four fingers – a measure used to determine the amount of silk that is pardoned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the maximum amount of silk a man may wear without being sinful?
A) Any amount is haram.
B) Up to four fingers measured with fingers spread.
C) Up to four fingers measured with clenched fists.
D) Up to the size of a palm.

Key Takeaway
Wearing silk is haram for men, but a small amount (four fingers' width) is excepted and permissible.

Using Silk as a Pillow or Sitting on It: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sitting on silk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on using silk for sitting or sleeping, and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits sitting on silk; the Sahibayn deem it disliked.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: may a man use silk as a pillow or sit on a silk cloth? The passage presents the views of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, along with their respective proofs. Understanding this disagreement refines your ability to apply the general prohibition of silk to non-wearing contexts.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa holds that using silk as a pillow (tawassud) or sitting on it (iftirash) is halal (mubah).
This is based on the principle that the prohibition is only for wearing (libs).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it makruh (disliked). They are joined by the three other imams (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad).
Muhammad's view is recorded as 'akrah' (I dislike).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Sahibayn cite the hadith of Hudhayfah: the Prophet forbade sitting on silk. This is narrated by Bukhari.
The hadith includes 'and from sitting on it' in the prohibition.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds with two proofs: (a) the Prophet himself used a silk cushion, and (b) the prohibition is specifically for 'wearing', not for other forms of use.
The Prophet's action overrides the general statement in the hadith according to Hanafi usul.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view: permissibility. This is reported in al-Jami' al-Saghir and supported by later texts.
Some later scholars note that Abu Yusuf may have agreed with Abu Hanifa, strengthening the majority position.

Similarly, sleeping on it is permissible according to him, and making it a curtain or hanging it on the door is a disagreement with them. In Nawadir Hisham, Muhammad said: I dislike a waistband (tikka) of dibaj and ibrisam (silk thread). And in al-Fatawa al-Sughra: There is no harm in a silk waistband according to Abu Hanifa. (Ramz al-Haqaiq: 2/375) 'And wearing what has silk etc.' means what is stretched lengthwise in weaving, because the Companions wore such, and because a garment only becomes a garment through weaving, and weaving is by the weft, so it is considered; or we say: it does not become a garment except by both, so the effective cause is shared and the last of them is considered, which is the weft; and because the weft is what appears in sight, so consideration is given to what appears, not what is hidden. Know that it is permissible to wear what has silk warp and khazz weft in war and otherwise by consensus. (Fath, Ayni) 'Or khazz' is the name of a sea animal, then the garment made from its fur is called khazz. (Mulla Miskin) 'Its opposite is permissible only in war' means its opposite, i.e., when the weft is silk and the warp is cotton or khazz or other, it is not permissible except in war, because we mentioned that the consideration is given to the weft. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Raiq: 8/380) And this is also by consensus due to necessity, because it is more awe-inspiring and repels the harm of weapons = as mentioned.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the rulings of silk (harir) and related fabrics for men. It begins by stating that silk is haram for men but halal for women, based on a hadith. However, a small amount (four fingers' width) is pardoned. The exact measure of 'four fingers' is discussed: whether they are spread or natural. The passage then addresses several related issues: (1) Sitting on silk (tawassud and iftirash): Abu Hanifa permits it, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider it makruh. The former relies on a report that the Prophet used a silk cushion, and that the prohibition is specifically for wearing. (2) Wearing silk in war: Abu Hanifa forbids it even with a barrier, but the Sahibayn permit it in war as a concession, citing the need for a formidable appearance. (3) Fabrics with mixed fibers: If the warp is silk and the weft is cotton or khazz, wearing is permissible by consensus (as the weft is considered). If the opposite (silk weft, cotton warp), it is only permissible in war. (4) The definition of dibaj: It is pure silk (both warp and weft), whereas harir in this context refers to mixed fabric with silk warp and cotton weft. (5) Silk waistbands (tikka): There is a difference between Abu Hanifa (permissible) and Muhammad (disliked). The passage ends by reinforcing that the weft determines the ruling, and that wearing such mixed fabrics in war is permissible by consensus for necessity.

Key Terms
توسد (tawassud): Using something as a pillow or cushion.
افتراش (iftirāsh): Using something as a floor covering or mat to sit on.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the default ruling for a man sitting on a silk cushion?
A) Haram, because silk is forbidden for men.
B) Permissible, according to Abu Hanifa.
C) Permissible only if the cushion is less than four fingers in area.
D) Makruh, according to all Hanafis.

Key Takeaway
Using silk as a pillow or sitting on it is permissible for men according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Mixed Silk Fabrics: Warp and Weft Rulings and War Concession
Rule on mixed fabrics and war exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the weft determines the ruling on mixed silk fabrics, and state when such fabrics are permissible in war.

Core Idea
The weft (luhmah) determines whether a mixed fabric is treated as silk or not.

This lesson focuses on the fiqh of mixed silk fabrics. The Hanafi school gives decisive weight to the weft (luhmah) in determining the ruling. If the weft is non-silk, the fabric is permissible; if the weft is silk, it is haram except in war. The passage also explains the wisdom behind the war concession.

Guided Path
1
Understand the weft principle
The passage states that the weft (luhmah) is the primary determinant because it is what appears in view and is essential in weaving. The ruling follows the weft.
If weft is silk, fabric is treated as silk.
2
Apply to two scenarios
Scenario 1: Warp is silk, weft is cotton or khazz → permissible. Scenario 2: Warp is cotton, weft is silk → haram, except in war.
The opposite case (weft silk) is given the opposite ruling.
3
Note the war concession
Wearing silk-weft fabric is permissible in war by consensus, due to necessity – it is more awe-inspiring and protects against weapons.
This is a rukhsa (concession) applicable in battle.
4
Identify the consensus
The passage mentions that the permissibility in war for silk-weft fabric is by consensus (ijma). Also, khazz fabric (with silk warp and khazz weft) is permissible in war and peace by consensus.
Khazz is a type of fabric from sea animal fur.
5
Apply to real-world examples
Today, many men's suits have silk warp and cotton weft. Knowing the weft determines the ruling, such suits are permissible. A tie with silk weft would be haram except in war.
Always check the weft composition.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the rulings of silk (harir) and related fabrics for men. It begins by stating that silk is haram for men but halal for women, based on a hadith. However, a small amount (four fingers' width) is pardoned. The exact measure of 'four fingers' is discussed: whether they are spread or natural. The passage then addresses several related issues: (1) Sitting on silk (tawassud and iftirash): Abu Hanifa permits it, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider it makruh. (5) Silk waistbands (tikka): There is a difference between Abu Hanifa (permissible) and Muhammad (disliked). The passage ends by reinforcing that the weft determines the ruling, and that wearing such mixed fabrics in war is permissible by consensus for necessity.

Key Terms
سدى (sudā): The warp of a fabric – the threads that run lengthwise in weaving.
لحمة (luḥmah): The weft of a fabric – the threads that run widthwise and interlace with the warp.
خز (khazz): A fabric made from the fur of a sea animal called khazz; it is considered non-silk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Binding and Reattaching Teeth: Silver, Gold, and Fallen Teeth
Explains the Hanafi ruling on tooth binding and the intra-madhhab dispute on reattaching a fallen tooth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on binding a tooth with silver and gold, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on reattaching a fallen tooth.

Core Idea
Binding a tooth with silver is allowed; gold is not, and reattaching a fallen tooth is disputed among the Hanafi masters.

This passage from the Book of Karahiya addresses the permissibility of repairing a tooth using silver or gold, and the related issue of reattaching a fallen tooth. The Hanafi school permits silver but differs on gold, and the Imams also disagree on whether a fallen tooth can be reattached at all. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for giving accurate fatwa in dental cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling and its scope
The text begins by stating that binding the tooth is permissible with silver but not with gold. This is the general rule for an intact tooth.
This ruling applies whether the tooth is intact or loose; the dispute concerns a fallen tooth.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute on reattaching a fallen tooth
The commentator notes that Abu Hanifa (Imam) considers it makruh to reattach a fallen tooth, while Abu Yusuf sees no harm. Imam Muhammad also allows gold for reattachment.
Abu Hanifa's view is based on qiyas, while Muhammad cites a hadith.
3
Examine the proofs: Abu Hanifa's qiyas and Muhammad's hadith
Abu Hanifa argues that gold is prohibited for men and necessity is fulfilled by silver. Muhammad uses the hadith of 'Arfaja, where the Prophet ordered a gold nose due to smell from silver.
The hadith is about the nose, not the tooth, and may be specific to 'Arfaja.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the hadith
The commentator argues that the incident of 'Arfaja is specific to the nose and does not generalize to teeth. Moreover, silver sufficed for the tooth, unlike the nose which had smell issues.
This response defends the prohibition of gold for teeth.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
Practical difference: if a tooth falls, some Hanafis consider it makruh to reattach with silver; others allow it. Gold is rejected by the majority. The text's initial ruling 'لا بالذهب' indicates the relied-upon position is that gold is not allowed.
Thus, the operative fatwa is that only silver is permitted for tooth binding.

إذا تحرك
ومخاط والرتم.

من
شد السن إلخ ظاهر إطلاقه عدم الفرق بين ما إذا شدها قبل السقوط أو بعده، وهو قول أبي يوسف، وعند الإمام يكره شدها بعد السقوط، قال في "الخانية": وإن سقطت ثنية الرجل، قال أبو حنيفة: يكره أن يعيدها ويشدها، ولكن يأخذ سن شاة ذكية ويشدها مكانها، وقال أبو يوسف لا بأس بأن يشد ثنيته في موضعها. (فتح) لا بالذهب: لأن المحرم لا يباح إلا للضرورة، وهي تندفع بالفضة، وهذا عندهما، وقال محمد: لا بأس بالذهب أيضا، وهو رواية عنهما لمحمد أن عرفجة بن سعد أصيب أنفه كلاب، فاتخذ أنفا فضة، فأنتن، فأمره النبي عليه أن يتخذ أنفا من ذهب، و به قالت الثلاثة، قلنا: الكلام في السن والمروي في الأنف، ولا يلزم من عدم الإغناء في الأنف عدم الإغناء في السن، على أنه يحتمل أن عرفجة قد خص به، قال في "الشرنبلالية" عن "المواهب": ذكر أن الخلاف في شد السن، أما اتخاذ الأنف من الذهب، فلا خلاف في جوازه، لكن ذكر الإسبيجابي الاختلاف في الأنف أيضا. و كره إلباس الخ: لأنه لما حرم اللبس حرم الإلباس كالخمر لما حرم شربها حرم سقيها الصبي، وكذا الميتة والدم، وعند الثلاثة لا يكره [ رمز الحقائق: ۳۷۷/۲] لا الخرقة لوضوء لعموم ذلك بين الناس في عامة البلدان، وما رآه المؤمنون حسنا، فهو عند الله حسن، وفي "الجامع الصغير": يكره حمل الخرقة التي يمسح بها العرق؛ لأنها بدعة محدثة، وتشبه بزي الأعاجم، والأول هو الأصح. Book of Disliked Acts. What relates to clothing. And binding the tooth with silver, not with gold. And it is disliked to clothe a child with gold and silk, except the cloth for wudu. If it moves? Binding the tooth etc.: apparently its absolute statement indicates no difference between whether he binds it before it falls or after, and this is the statement of Abu Yusuf. According to Imam (Abu Hanifa) it is disliked to bind it after it falls.
