Abu Yusuf said there is no harm that he bind his incisor in its place. (Fath) Not with gold: because what is prohibited is not permitted except for necessity, and necessity is fulfilled with silver, and this is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And Muhammad said: There is no harm with gold as well, and it is a narration from them for Muhammad that 'Arfaja ibn Sa'd had his nose cut by dogs, so he made a nose of silver, and it stank, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered him to make a nose of gold, and the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad?) said that. We say: The discussion is about the tooth, and what is narrated is about the nose, and it does not follow from the insufficiency of silver in the nose the insufficiency in the tooth, unless it is possible that 'Arfaja was specifically addressed. He said in al-Shurnaballaliyya from al-Mawahib: It is mentioned that the disagreement is about binding the tooth, as for making a nose of gold, there is no disagreement on its permissibility, but al-Asbijabi mentioned the difference regarding the nose as well. (Fath, Mulla Miskin) Day? And it is disliked to clothe etc.: because when wearing became prohibited, giving to wear also became prohibited, like when drinking wine is prohibited, giving it to a child to drink is prohibited, and likewise carrion and blood. According to the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and others) it is not disliked. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/377] Not the cloth for wudu due to the prevalence of that among people in most countries, and whatever the believers deem good is good with Allah. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: It is disliked to carry the cloth with which one wipes sweat, because it is an innovated practice and resembles the dress of non-Arabs. And the first is more correct. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/377] And mucus: or sweat or for need, and if for arrogance it is disliked. And al-ratam etc.: i.e., al-ratam is also not disliked, and it is the 'rahima' and 'ratima', which is a string of remembrance tied on the finger; because it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon her? [error in text]) ordered some of his companions and a genuine purpose was attached to it, so it is not disliked. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/377] And it is unlike al-tasmiyah, which is a string that was tied around the neck or hand in the jahiliyyah to repel harm from themselves according to their claim, and it is prohibited, and in 'Hudud al-Iman' it is disbelief, due to the hadith: 'Incantations and amulets are shirk', and this is carried on the incantations of jahiliyyah, because they used to recite words of disbelief. (Fath)
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on rulings from the Book of Karahiya concerning bodily adornment and clothing. The author covers four topics: (1) binding a tooth with silver is permissible, with gold is not; (2) making a child wear gold or silk is disliked except a small cloth for wudu; (3) using a cloth to wipe sweat is disliked; (4) amulets: the 'ratam' (a string tied for remembrance) is permissible, while the 'tasmiyah' (pagan amulet) is prohibited and considered kufr. The text also presents intra-Hanafi disagreements: Abu Hanifa dislikes reattaching a fallen tooth, while Abu Yusuf allows it; Imam Muhammad allows gold for the tooth based on the hadith of 'Arfaja, but the author argues that the nose case is specific. The ruling on cloth for wudu is based on general custom in Muslim lands. The amulet discussion distinguishes between harmless remembrance strings and pagan talismans.

Key Terms
شد السن (shadd al-sinn): Binding or repairing a tooth with a metal wire or replacement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on reattaching a fallen tooth?
A) Permissible (ja'iz)
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Forbidden (haram)
D) Recommended (mustahab)

Key Takeaway
Binding a tooth with silver is permissible; gold is prohibited. Reattaching a fallen tooth is a point of legitimate ikhtilaf, with the relied-upon view allowing it.

Clothing Children with Gold and Silk: Disliked Act with an Exception
Explains the Hanafi ruling on clothing children with gold/silk and the exception for the wudu cloth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why dressing a child in gold and silk is disliked (makruh) and identify the exception made for a small cloth used for wudu.

Core Idea
Dressing a child in gold or silk is disliked because it involves what is haram for men; a wudu cloth is exempt due to widespread custom.

This passage addresses the ruling on giving a child gold or silk garments. Since gold and silk are haram for men, providing them to a child is considered makruh tanzihi. However, a small cloth used for wudu is excepted based on 'urf. The text also contrasts this with a cloth for wiping sweat, which is disliked as an innovation.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its underlying qiyas
The text rules that clothing a child with gold or silk is disliked. The reason is qiyas: just as giving wine to a child is prohibited, giving gold/silk is similarly prohibited because the act enables haram.
The child is not accountable, but the adult is responsible for facilitating prohibition.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
According to the 'three' (likely Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and another), it is not disliked. This shows that the ruling is not unanimous within the school.
The relied-upon view is that it is disliked, as per the matn.
3
Identify the exception for the wudu cloth
The exception 'لا الخرقة لوضوء' is based on widespread practice among Muslims. The hadith principle: 'whatever the believers deem good is good with Allah' supports this.
Custom ('urf) can specify a ruling when no explicit text contradicts.
4
Distinguish from the cloth for wiping sweat
In al-Jami' al-Saghir, carrying a cloth to wipe sweat is disliked because it is an innovation and resembles non-Muslims. This ruling is contrasted with the wudu cloth.
The rule is: exception for common practice; otherwise disliked.

إذا تحرك
ومخاط والرتم.

(فتح) لا بالذهب: لأن المحرم لا يباح إلا للضرورة، وهي تندفع بالفضة، وهذا عندهما، وقال محمد: لا بأس بالذهب أيضا، وهو رواية عنهما لمحمد أن عرفجة بن سعد أصيب أنفه كلاب، فاتخذ أنفا فضة، فأنتن، فأمره النبي عليه أن يتخذ أنفا من ذهب، و به قالت الثلاثة، قلنا: الكلام في السن والمروي في الأنف، ولا يلزم من عدم الإغناء في الأنف عدم الإغناء في السن، على أنه يحتمل أن عرفجة قد خص به، قال في "الشرنبلالية" عن "المواهب": ذكر أن الخلاف في شد السن، أما اتخاذ الأنف من الذهب، فلا خلاف في جوازه، لكن ذكر الإسبيجابي الاختلاف في الأنف أيضا. (فتح، ملا مسكين)
يوم

[ رمز الحقائق: ۳۷۷/۲] وهي بخلاف التسمية، وهي خيط كان يربط في العنق أو في اليد في الجاهلية؛ لدفع المضرة عن أنفسهم على زعمهم، وهو منهي عنه، وفي حدود الإيمان" أنه كفر ، لحديث: "أن الرقى والتمائم شرك"، وهذا محمول على رقى الجاهلية؛ إذ كانوا يرقون بكلمات الكفر. Amulets: Permissible Ratam vs. Prohibited Tasmiyah
Explains the Hanafi ruling on two types of strings: permissible ratam and prohibited tasmiyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the permissible ratam (reminder string) and the prohibited tasmiyah (pagan amulet) in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A string tied for remembrance (ratam) is allowed; a pagan amulet for protection (tasmiyah) is shirk and kufr.

This passage clarifies the ruling on strings tied on the body. The term 'ratam' refers to a string tied on the finger as a reminder for tasks, which is permissible. In contrast, 'tasmiyah' (also called tamimah) is a pagan practice of tying strings for protection, which is prohibited and considered kufr due to the hadith 'Incantations and amulets are shirk'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of strings
The text mentions 'ratam' (a string for remembrance) and 'tasmiyah' (a jahili string for protection). They are different in purpose and ruling.
Ratam is also called 'rahima' or 'ratima'.
2
State the ruling for ratam
Ratam is not disliked. It is based on a report that the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered some companions to use it for a genuine purpose.
Because it serves a valid purpose, it is permissible.
3
State the ruling for tasmiyah
Tasmiyah is prohibited and considered kufr. It is a jahili practice of tying strings to repel harm.
The hadith declares such amulets as shirk.
4
Understand the distinction based on intention
The difference lies in the intention: ratam is for remembering tasks, not for protection. Tasmiyah is superstitious and relies on the string for benefit.
This aligns with the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'.

Source Text

10 min
Rulings on Looking at Non-Mahram Women
Details the Hanafi ruling on permissible looking at non-mahram women with proofs and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking at a non-mahram free woman, identify the conditions and exceptions, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding the foot.

Core Idea
Looking at a non-mahram free woman's face and hands is permissible without desire; all else is forbidden.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq details the Hanafi position on permissible and impermissible looking between genders. It begins with the general rule that a man may only look at the face and hands of a free woman, provided he is free from desire. The text then discusses exceptions for a judge and a marriage proposer, the prohibition of seclusion, and a difference of opinion regarding the foot. Understanding these rulings is essential for maintaining proper social conduct in accordance with the Shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Determine what parts of a free woman a man may look at and under what condition.
The text states 'لا ينظر إلى غير وجه الحرة وكفيها' – only face and hands.
2
Understand the condition of desire
Note that permissibility is conditional on absence of desire; if desire is present or expected, looking becomes haram.
The text says 'هذا إن لم يكن النظر عن شهوة' – permissibility only without desire.
3
Recognize the exceptions
Identify who is allowed to look even with desire and why.
The judge and the marriage proposer are exceptions due to necessity.
4
Analyze the foot dispute
Examine the differing views on whether the foot is 'awrah for looking and touching.
Al-Hidayah states the preferred view is that it is not 'awrah; Abu Yusuf also allows forearms.
5
Connect to related rulings
Note that seclusion with a non-mahram is forbidden, and touching face/hands is prohibited even without desire.
The text equates prohibition of looking with prohibition of khalwa and touching.

كتاب الكراهية
٣٣٦
ما يتعلق النظر واللمس
فصل في النظر واللمس
لا ينظر إلى غير وجه الحرة وكفيها ولا ينظر من اشتهى إلى وجهها إلا الحاكم
فصل في النظر إلخ لما أنهى الكلام على مسائل ،اللبس وقدمه؛ لشدة الاحتياج إليه، ذكر بعده مسائل النظر؛ لأنها أكثر وقوعا من مسائل الاستبراء؛ فلذا قدمها، ومسائل النظر أقسام أربعة نظر الرجل إلى المرأة، ونظر المرأة إلى الرجل، ونظر الرجل إلى الرجل، ونظر المرأة إلى المرأة، والقسم الأول منها على أربعة أقسام: نظر الرجل إلى الأجنبية، ونظره إلى زوجته وأمته ونظره إلى ذوات محارمه ونظره إلى أمة الغير، وبين المصنف أحكامها، وبدأ بالقسم الأول من أقسام نظر الرجل إلى المرأة تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٨٣/٨]
لا ينظر إلى غير إلخ أي لا يجوز النظر إلى غير وجه الحرة ،وكفيها وليس في كلام المصنف التحريض على النظر إلى هذين العضوين كما ادعاه الزيلعي واعلم أن إطلاق قول المصنف: "لا ينظر إلى غير وجه الحرة" إلخ يتناول لما إذا كان الناظر للحرة ،عبدها، وبه صرح في الدرر" حيث قال لمملوكها أن ينظر إلى وجه سيدته وكفيها لا قدميها وسيذكره المصنف آخر هذا الفصل بقوله: "وعبدها كالأجنـ أجنبي". فتح ا الله المعين) يجوز له أن ينظر إلى وجه الحرة وكفيها سواء كانت شابة أو عجوزا وإنما جاز النظر إليها؛ لقوله تعالى: وَلا يُبْدِينَ زِينَتَهُنَّ إِ نَّ إِلَّا مَا ظَهَرَ مِنْهَا﴾ (النور: (۳۱) قال علي وابن عباس : "ما ظهر منها الكحل والخاتم"، والمراد به موضعهما، وهو الوجه والكف كما أريد بالزينة محلها، ولأن في إبدائهما ضرورة؛ لحاجتها إلى المعاملة مع الرجال والإعطاء وغير ذلك من المخالطة كالمشي في الطريق، وكما لا يجوز النظر فكذا الخلوة. قال في "الدر" عن الأشباه الخلوة بالأجنبية حرام إلا لملازمة مديونة هربت ودخلت خربة وفيما إذا كانت عجوزا شوهاء وفيما إذا كان بينهما حائل وفيه عنها الخلوة بالمحرم مباحة إلا الأخت من الرضاعة والصهرة الشابة، وما عدا ما استثنى من الأعضاء لا يجوز له أن ينظر إليه؛ لقوله عليه "من نظر إلى محاسن امرأة عن شهوة صب في القيامة"، ولا يحل له أن يمس وجهها ولا كفيها وإن كان يأمن الشهوة، هذا أي جواز النظر الأنك يوم إذا لم يكن النظر عن شهوة، فإن كان يعلم أنه إن نظر اشتهى لم يحل النظر إلى شيء منها. (فتح) وكفيها فيه إشارة إلى أن القدم ،عورة، وفيه خلاف، ففي الهداية": الأصح أنه ليس بعورة، وقيل: الصحيح أنه عورة في حق النظر والمس، وليس بعورة في حق الصلاة والمشي وعن أبي يوسف أنه يباح النظر إلى ذراعيها؛ لأنه يبدو منها عادة. (عيني، فتح) ولا ينظر إلخ أراد أن صاحب الشهوة لا يجوز له أن ينظر إلى وجهها أي وجه الحرة مع جواز النظر إليه عند عدم الشهوة رمز الحقائق: ۳۷۸/۲۸] إلا الحاكم إلخ : [ وكذا مريد نكاحها ولو عن شهوة بنية السنة. (ط) فإنهما ينظران مطلقا للضرورة في إحياء الحقوق، والأصل أنه لا يجوز أن ينظر إلى وجه الأجنبية بشهوة لما روينا؛ إلا للضرورة إذا تيقن بالشهوة أو شك فيها، وفي نظر من ذكرنا مع الشهوة ضرورة =
عينيه
Book of Disliked Acts. 336. What relates to looking and touching. Chapter on looking and touching. He does not look at other than the face and palms of a free woman, and he who desires should not look at her face except the judge. Chapter on looking etc. When he finished speaking on matters of clothing and presented it due to the severe need for it, he mentioned after it matters of looking because they occur more frequently than matters of istibra'; for this reason he presented them. Matters of looking are four divisions: looking of a man at a woman, looking of a woman at a man, looking of a man at a man, and looking of a woman at a woman. The first division among them is of four types: looking of a man at a strange woman, his looking at his wife and his slave-girl, his looking at his mahrams, and his looking at another's slave-girl. The author explained their rulings and began with the first division of the types of a man's looking at a woman. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 8/383). He does not look at other than etc., i.e., it is not permissible to look at other than the face and the two palms of a free woman. There is not in the author's speech an incitement to look at these two parts as al-Zayla'i claimed. Know that the absolute statement of the author: 'He does not look at other than the face of a free woman' includes the case when the looker is her slave. And it is explicitly stated in al-Durar where it said: Her slave may look at the face and hands of his mistress, not her feet. And the author will mention it at the end of this chapter with his statement: 'And her slave is like a stranger.' (Fath al-Mu'in) It is permissible for him to look at the face and hands of a free woman, whether she is young or old. It is only permissible to look at her because of His statement: 'And they should not display their adornment except what appears of it' (Quran 24:31). 'Ali and Ibn 'Abbas said: 'What appears of it is the kohl and the ring,' and by that is meant their place, which is the face and the palm, as the adornment's location is intended, and because displaying them is a necessity due to her need for dealing with men and giving and other mixing like walking on the road. And just as looking is impermissible, so is seclusion. He said in al-Dur from al-Ashbah: Seclusion with a strange woman is forbidden except for following a female debtor who fled and entered a ruin, and when she is an ugly old woman, and when there is a barrier between them. In it, seclusion with a mahram is permissible except for a foster sister and a young in-law? And other than the excepted parts, it is not permissible for him to look at them due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever looks at the beauty of a woman with desire, molten lead will be poured into his eyes on the Day of Resurrection.' And it is not permissible for him to touch her face or hands even if he is safe from desire. This, i.e., the permissibility of looking, is only when the looking is without desire. If he knows that if he looks he will desire, it is not permissible to look at anything of her. (Fath) And 'her two palms' in it indicate that the foot is 'awrah, and there is a difference. In al-Hidayah: The more correct is that it is not 'awrah, and it is said: The correct is that it is 'awrah regarding looking and touching, and not 'awrah regarding prayer and walking. From Abu Yusuf, it is permissible to look at her forearms because they usually appear. ('Ayni, Fath) And he does not look etc. He intended that the man of desire is not permitted to look at her face, i.e., the face of the free woman, while looking at it is permissible when there is no desire (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/378). Except the judge etc.: and likewise the one who wants to marry her, even with desire with the intention of sunnah. (T) For both of them look absolutely due to necessity in reviving rights. The origin is that it is not permissible to look at the face of a strange woman with desire based on what we narrate, except for necessity if he is certain of desire or doubts it. And in the looking of those we mentioned with desire, there is necessity = 'Aynaihi?
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rulings of looking at non-mahram women. The author begins by stating the general rule: a man may look only at the face and hands of a free woman, provided he is free of desire. Looking with desire is prohibited. Exceptions are made for a judge and a marriage proposer, who may look even with desire due to necessity. The text also discusses seclusion (khalwa) with a non-mahram, which is generally forbidden. There is a difference of opinion regarding the status of the foot: some consider it 'awrah, while the preferred view in al-Hidayah is that it is not. The author emphasizes that the permissibility is conditional on absence of desire. Additionally, the text clarifies that a slave may look at his mistress's face and hands, and that touching the face and hands is impermissible even without desire.

Key Terms
الوجه والكفان (al-wajh wa al-kuffan): The face and palms of a free woman, which are permissible to look at without desire.
الخلوة (al-khalwa): Seclusion of a man and woman who are not mahram, which is prohibited in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, who is allowed to look at a free woman's face even with desire?
A) The judge and the marriage proposer.
B) Any man in the street.
C) Her brother.
D) No one is allowed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits looking at the face and hands of a non-mahram free woman without desire, with exceptions for necessity, and the foot is not considered 'awrah for looking.

Permissible Looking for Necessity: Medical and Judicial
Rulings on permissible looking for necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which looking at another's 'awrah is permissible for medical or judicial necessity and apply those conditions to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Looking at 'awrah is permissible for genuine necessity, with strict conditions to avoid desire.

The Hanafi school balances the prohibition of exposing the 'awrah with the need for medical treatment, judicial proceedings, and other essential purposes. This lesson examines the rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq on when a physician, judge, witness, or practitioner may look at otherwise prohibited body parts. The core principle is necessity (darurah), but the permissibility is tightly regulated to prevent abuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
Recognise that the passage permits looking at the 'awrah for specific necessities (medical, judicial, testimony, cupping, circumcision, extreme emaciation). The basis is the general principle that necessity permits what is normally prohibited (al-darurah tubihu al-mahzurat).
This is not unrestricted permission; it is limited to the extent of the necessity.
2
List the conditions for each case
Extract the conditions mentioned: for a physician, look only at the site of illness, cover other body parts, lower gaze, and if possible, use a female assistant. For judge/witness, intend only the legal purpose, avoid desire, and at the time of taking testimony (tahammul) looking with desire is absolutely prohibited.
The conditions vary by role but all centre on necessity and the absence of lust.
3
Analyse the 'awrah hierarchy
Note the degrees of prohibition for male 'awrah: knee (light rebuke), thigh (harsh rebuke), private parts (beating if persistent). This informs how much exposure is tolerated even within the 'awrah.
This hierarchy affects the level of strictness when necessity requires exposure of different parts.
4
Apply the rule to analogous cases
The text extrapolates from the physician to cupper, circumciser, and even extreme emaciation. Understand that the illah (effective cause) is necessity to preserve health or fulfil a legal duty. Use this same reasoning to extend to modern equivalents (e.g., X-ray technician).
The ruling is derived via talil (reasoning by cause), not direct textual evidence for each case.

Source Text

كتاب الكراهية
۳۳۷
ما يتعلق النظر واللمس
والشاهد، وينظر الطبيب إلى موضع مرضها وينظر الرجل إلى الرجل إلا العورة
إذا أرادا الحكم والشهادة وكذا نظر قابلة وختان فقط
أي وتنظر اللام بمعنى إلى
وإلى سائر بدنها التي يحل له وطئها
، فيجوز، وكذا نظر الحاقنة والحاقن، وكذا نظر الخاتن إذا أراد أن يداوي مع الختان، وكذا يجوز النظر للهزال الفاحش؛ لأنه أمارة المرض، ولكن يجب على القاضي والشاهد قصد أداء الشهادة والحكم لا قضاء الشهوة تحرزا عن القبيح بقدر الإمكان، وهذا وقت الأداء، وأما وقت التحمل، فلا يجوز أن ينظر إليها مع الشهوة؛ لأنه يوجد من لا يشتهي، فلا حاجة إليه. (فتح، تكملة، عيني) وينظر الطبيب إلخ: للضرورة، ولكن يستر كل عضو منها سوى موضع المرض، ثم ينظر ويغض بصره عن غير ذلك الموضع ما استطاع، وينبغي أن يعلم امرأة إن أمكن لأن نظر الجنس أخف، وإلا ينظر على الوجه الذي ذكرناه رمز الحقائق: ۳۷۸/۲] إلا العورة: أي لا ينظر الرجل إلى عورة الرجل، وهي ما بين السرة إلى الركبة، والسرة ليست من العورة والركبة منها، وإنما لم ينبه المؤلف هنا لما قدم في "كتاب الصلاة"، وقد بينا الدليل هناك، وحكم العورة في الركبة أخف منه في الفخذ وفي الفخذ أخف منه في السوأة حتى ينكر عليه في كشف الركبة برفق، وفي الفخذ بعنف، وفي السوأة بضرب إن لج. [تكملة البحر الرائق: ٣٨٦/٨]
Book of Disliked Things. What pertains to looking and touching. And the witness, and the physician looks at the place of her illness, and a man looks at a man except for the ‘awrah if they intend judgment and testimony, and likewise the look of a midwife and a circumciser only. That is, and she looks, the lām meaning ‘at’, and at the rest of her body which it is permissible for him to have intercourse with. So it is permissible. And likewise the look of the cupper and the one being cupped, and likewise the look of the circumciser if he intends to treat along with circumcision, and likewise looking is permissible for extreme emaciation because it is a sign of illness. But it is obligatory upon the judge and the witness to intend to perform testimony and judgment, not to fulfill desire, guarding against the improper as much as possible. This is at the time of performance. As for the time of taking testimony, it is not permissible to look at her with desire because there exists one who does not desire, so there is no need. (Fath, Takmila, ‘Ayni) 'And the physician etc.': due to necessity, but he must cover every part of her except the place of illness, then look and lower his gaze from other than that place as much as he can, and it is recommended that he instruct a woman if possible because looking at one’s gender is lighter, otherwise he looks in the manner we mentioned. (Ramz al-Haqa’iq 2:378) 'Except the ‘awrah': meaning a man does not look at the ‘awrah of a man, which is between the navel and the knee. The navel is not from the ‘awrah and the knee is from it. The author did not alert here because he presented earlier in the Book of Prayer, and we have explained the proof there. The ruling of the ‘awrah in the knee is lighter than in the thigh, and in the thigh lighter than in the private parts, such that one is rebuked gently for uncovering the knee, harshly for the thigh, and beaten if persisting for the private parts. (Takmilat al-Bahr al-Ra’iq 8:386)
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq, with commentary, addresses the ruling on looking at others’ private parts and bodies for necessary purposes. The matn states that a physician may look at the site of a woman patient’s illness, a judge and witness may look at a person (excluding the ‘awrah) for judgment and testimony, and a midwife and circumciser may look for their work. The commentary explains that the physician may look at any part of a woman he is permitted to have intercourse with, meaning his wife or slave woman; for others, only the site of illness. Looking is also allowed for cupping, circumcision treatment, and in case of extreme emaciation as a sign of illness. However, the judge and witness must intend only the legal purpose and avoid desire; during taking testimony (tahammul), looking with desire is not allowed at all. The physician must cover all of the patient’s body except the needed site, lower his gaze, and preferably use a female assistant. The ‘awrah of a man is between navel and knee, with varying degrees of prohibition.

Key Terms
عورة (‘awrah): The parts of the body that must be covered; for a man, between navel and knee (knee included, navel not).
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity that permits normally prohibited actions, but only to the extent needed.
تحمل (tahammul): The process of taking testimony from a witness before a judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a physician looking at a woman patient?
A) It is forbidden under all circumstances.
B) It is permissible only for the site of illness, with covering other parts and lowering gaze.
C) It is permissible only if the patient is his wife.
D) It is permissible only if no female physician is available.

Key Takeaway
Looking at the 'awrah for genuine medical or judicial necessity is permissible in the Hanafi school, provided all conditions to minimize exposure and avoid desire are met.

Rules of Looking at Non-Mahram Men and Women
Rulings on looking between same gender and non-mahram opposite gender

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on looking at non-mahram members of the same and opposite gender, and apply the distinction between when lowering the gaze is obligatory versus recommended.

Core Idea
Looking at non-mahram same gender is permissible; opposite gender depends on presence of desire.

This lesson clarifies the rules of looking between individuals who are not mahram. The default ruling for men looking at men and women looking at women is permissibility of non-‘awrah parts, but the presence of desire changes the ruling. A key difference between men and women is highlighted: for men, desire triggers an obligation to lower the gaze; for women, it is only recommended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties involved
Determine whether the looker and the one being looked at are of the same gender or opposite gender, and whether they are mahram or non-mahram.
The text focuses on non-mahram opposite gender and same gender.
2
State the default ruling
The default ruling for looking at a non-mahram of the same gender is permissibility of looking at the entire body except the 'awrah. This applies when both parties are safe from desire and temptation.
3
Apply the condition of desire
If desire is present or reasonably suspected, the ruling changes. The text distinguishes between a man's desire and a woman's desire.
4
Apply the gender-specific ruling
For a man: if he feels desire, it is obligatory (wajib) to lower his gaze. For a woman: if she feels desire or even doubts, it is recommended (mustahabb) to lower her gaze. The reasoning is that desire from both sides is stronger than from one side.
5
Note the extension after death
The prohibition of looking at certain body parts remains even after death. For example, the pubic hair, head hair, forearm bone, and shin of a free woman remain forbidden to look at even after death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمرأة إلخ: معناه: المرأة في النظر إلى المرأة والرجل كالرجل في النظر إلى الرجل يعني كما ينظر الرجل إلى الرجل، وهو جواز النظر إلى جميع البدن غير العورة، وهذا إذا أمنت الشهوة والفتن وإن كان في قلبها شهوة، أو في أكبر رأيها أنها تشتهي أو شكت في ذلك، يستحب لها أن تغض بصرها، بخلاف الرجل عند الشهوة، يجب عليه أن لا ينظر والفرق أن الشهوة غالبة عليهن وهي كالمتحقق حكما، فإذا اشتهى الرجل كانت الشهوة موجودة من الجانبين، وإذا اشتهت هي لم توجد إلا منها، فكانت من جانب واحد، والموجود من الجانبين أقوى، وما جاز للرجل أن ينظر إليه جاز مسه؛ لأنه ليس بعورة، بخلاف مسه في المرأة، وكل عضو لا يجوز النظر إليه قبل الانفصال لا يجوز بعده ولو بعد الموت كشعر عانته وشعر رأسها وعظم ذراع حرة ميتة وساقها، وقلامة ظفر رجلها دون يدها. (تكملة، فتح، عيني) والرجل: أي تنظر المرأة إلى الرجل أيضا كالرجل إلخ أي كنظر الرجل الرجل فلا تنظر المرأة إلى ما بين سرة المرأة. (ط)
And the woman etc.: its meaning: a woman in looking at a woman and a man is like a man in looking at a man, meaning just as a man looks at a man, which is the permissibility of looking at the entire body except the ‘awrah. This is when one is safe from desire and temptation. Even if there is desire in her heart, or in her best opinion she desires or she doubts that, it is recommended for her to lower her gaze. Unlike a man, when there is desire, it is obligatory for him not to look. The difference is that desire is predominant in women and it is as if established by law. So if a man desires, desire exists from both sides; if she desires, it exists only from her, so it is from one side. What exists from both sides is stronger. And what is permissible for a man to look at, it is permissible for him to touch because it is not ‘awrah, unlike touching her. And every limb that is not permissible to look at before separation is not permissible after, even after death, like her pubic hair, hair of her head, bone of a free dead woman’s forearm and her shin, and the nail paring of her foot but not her hand. (Takmila, Fath, ‘Ayni) And the man: meaning a woman also looks at a man like a man etc., i.e., like a man looking at a man, so a woman does not look at what is between the navel of a woman. (T)
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This commentary explains that women may look at other women and at non-mahram men just as men may look at other men, i.e., only the parts that are not ‘awrah. However, if a woman feels desire or fears temptation, it becomes recommended (mustahabb) for her to lower her gaze. For a man, if he feels desire, it is obligatory (wajib) to lower his gaze because desire from both sides is stronger. The commentary also states that any body part that is forbidden to look at during life remains forbidden after death (e.g., pubic hair, hair of the head, forearm bone, shin). It also distinguishes the permissibility of touching: what is allowed to look at is allowed to touch, except for a woman (i.e., a man may touch a woman only if she is his wife or mahram).

Key Terms
شهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire; if present, looking may become haram or makruh for men and women.
يستحب (yustaḥabb): Recommended (mustahabb); an act whose performance is rewarded but omission is not sinful.
يجب (yajib): Obligatory (wajib); an act whose omission is sinful, though not at the level of fard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a woman become recommended to lower her gaze from a non-mahram man?
A) Only if she is certain of desire.
B) If she feels desire or is uncertain about it.
C) At all times, like a man.
D) Only if she is alone with him.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on looking at non-mahram depends on the presence of desire: for men it becomes obligatory to lower the gaze, for women it is recommended.

Definition and Ruling of Istibra' for a Purchased Slave Woman
Definition and obligation of istibra' for a purchased slave woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of istibra', explain the acts prohibited until its completion, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding its necessity when the womb is certainly empty.

Core Idea
Istibra' is obligatory even if the womb is certainly empty — the prohibition of intercourse until completion protects against mixing lineages.

This passage defines istibra' (the waiting period to confirm the womb is empty) and establishes the ruling that a purchaser of a slave woman must complete it before any sexual access. We will examine the textual definition, the evidence from the hadith of Autas, and a subtle difference among the Hanafi imams regarding cases of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Define istibra' linguistically and legally
From the text: linguistically, 'seeking freedom'; legally, 'seeking freedom of the womb from pregnancy' by waiting for a specified period.
2
Identify the prohibited acts before istibra'
Intercourse, touching, and looking at the private part with desire are forbidden until istibra' is completed.
The prohibition is absolute, covering all forms of intimate contact.
3
State the duration of istibra' based on the woman's condition
One menstrual cycle for menstruating women, one month for those past menopause, and until delivery for pregnant women.
These are the default periods; any uncertainty about completion requires waiting.
4
Analyze the evidence (dalil) from the hadith
The Prophet said: 'Indeed, pregnant women should not be approached until they deliver, and non-pregnant ones until they have menstruated once.' This indicates obligation via the prohibition.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in practice.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf exempts istibra' if the buyer is certain the womb is empty; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require it unconditionally. The relied-upon view is the general obligation.
The majority hold that the text's generality overrides personal certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستبراء وغيره من ملك أمة حرم وطئها ولمسها والنظر إلى فرجها بشهوة حتى تستبرئ، والمجبوب: هو مقطوع الذكر والخصيتين من جب إذا قطع كالفحل أي في النظر إلى الأجنبية. فصل في الاستبراء إلخ: الاستبراء في اللغة: طلب البراءة، وفي الشرع : طلب براءة الرحم من الحمل. (عيني، فتح) أمة: أي استمتاع أمة فيخرج شراء الرجل زوجته. (فتح) حرم وطئها إلخ أي مطلقا سواء تيقن فراغ رحمها من ماء البائع بأن يكون مالكها امرأة أو صبيا، أو تكون الجارية بكرا أو نحوه، أو لم يتيقن، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه إذا تيقن فراغ رحمها من ماء البائع، فليس عليه الاستبراء. ملا) (مسكين حتى تستبرئ إلخ: بأن يتركها إلى أن تحيض حيضة واحدة إن كانت من ذوات الحيض، أو يمضي عليها شهر واحد إن كانت آئسة، أو إلى أن تضع حملها إن كانت حاملا؛ لقوله عليه السلام في سبايا أوطاس: ألا لا توطأ الحبالى حتى يضعن حملهن، ولا الحيالي حتى يستبرأن بحيضة"، وهذا يفيد وجوب الاستبراء بسبب إحداث الملك واليد حتى يجب على المشتري من مال الصبي ومن المرأة.
Chapter on istibra' and other matters: Whoever owns a female slave, intercourse with her, touching her, and looking at her private part with desire are forbidden until she undergoes istibra'. And the maimed (majbub) is one whose penis and testicles are cut... [section heading] Chapter on istibra': Istibra' linguistically means seeking freedom; legally: seeking freedom of the womb from pregnancy. (Ayni, Fath) 'A slave woman': i.e., [the prohibition applies to] sexual enjoyment of a slave woman; thus purchasing a man's own wife is excluded. (Fath) 'Intercourse is forbidden' etc., i.e., absolutely, whether one is certain that her womb is free from the seller's fluid (because her owner was a woman or a child, or the slave girl is a virgin or the like) or not certain. From Abu Yusuf: If one is certain that her womb is free from the seller's fluid, then there is no istibra' upon him. 'Until she performs istibra'': i.e., he leaves her until she menstruates once if she is of menstruating age, or one month passes if she is past menopause, or until she delivers if she is pregnant. Due to the Prophet's saying regarding the captives of Autas: 'Indeed, pregnant women should not be approached until they deliver, and non-pregnant ones until they have menstruated once.' This indicates the obligation of istibra' due to the new ownership and possession, so it is required even for the purchaser from the property of a child or a woman.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of istibra' (waiting period to ensure the womb is empty) for a newly purchased slave woman. The matn states that until istibra' is completed, intercourse, touching, and looking at her private parts with desire are forbidden. The commentary defines istibra' linguistically and legally: it is the process of confirming that the womb is free from pregnancy, typically by waiting for one menstrual cycle (for menstruating women), one month (for those past menopause), or until delivery (if pregnant). The evidence is the Prophet's prohibition in the hadith about the captives of Autas. The commentary also notes that the prohibition applies even if the buyer is certain the womb is empty, according to the general ruling, but Abu Yusuf excludes cases of certainty. Additionally, the ruling applies to all purchasers, even if buying from a minor or a woman.

Key Terms
استبراء (istibra'): Legally: seeking freedom of the womb from pregnancy by waiting for one menstruation, one month, or delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How must a purchased slave woman who has passed menopause perform istibra'?
A) She must wait for one menstrual cycle
B) She must wait for one lunar month
C) She must wait until she gives birth
D) No istibra' is required for her

Key Takeaway
Istibra' is an obligatory waiting period upon purchase of a slave woman, completed by one menstruation, one month, or delivery, before any intimate contact is permitted.

Ruling on Embracing in One Garment and Handshake
Clarifies permissibility of embracing and handshake

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on embracing in one garment and explain its analogy to handshake.

Core Idea
Embracing in one garment with a shirt is permissible without dislike, just like the sunnah of handshake.

This passage addresses the permissibility of embracing another person while sharing a single garment. The author clarifies that this act is not disliked, provided that at least one person is wearing a shirt. The ruling is established by analogy to handshake, which is a confirmed sunnah passed down from the early Muslim community. We will examine the ruling, its condition, and the broader context of physical greetings in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is embracing ('inaq) another person while both are covered by a single garment (izar). The condition mentioned is that at least one person has a shirt on (in kana 'alayhi qamis).
The shirt (qamis) ensures a barrier, addressing concerns of skin contact.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is 'ja'iz' (permissible) and specifically 'bila karahah' (without dislike). The commentator emphasizes that there is no disapproval.
The absence of dislike is key; the act is not just permissible but also not disliked.
3
Understand the basis: analogy to handshake
The text analogizes this embrace to handshake (musafahah), saying 'ka'l-musafahah' (like the handshake). Handshake is described as a sunnah inherited from early Muslims, used in pledges and other contexts.
This analogy establishes the ruling as positive, not merely tolerated.
4
Note the sunnah form of handshake
A citation from al-Qunyah states that the sunnah in handshake is to use both hands. This adds a detail about the recommended manner.
Using both hands shows warmth and is not required for validity, but it is the ideal.

Source Text

ومعانقته في إزار واحد، وإن كان عليه قميص جاز كالمصافحة. بلا كراهة كالمصافحة أي كما يجوز المصافحة؛ لأنها سنة قديمة متوارثة في البيعة وغير ذلك، وفي 'القنية': السنة في المصافحة أن تكون بكلتا يديه رمز الحقائق: ۳۸۱/۲] (طائي) الخالصة
And embracing him in one garment, and if he has a shirt on, it is permissible like the handshake. Without dislike. 'Like the handshake' meaning just as the handshake is permissible, because it is an ancient inherited sunnah in the pledge and otherwise. And in al-Qunyah: The sunnah in handshake is to do it with both hands.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the permissibility of physical greeting gestures. The author states that embracing another person while both are covered by a single garment is allowed without dislike, provided that at least one of them is wearing a shirt. The commentator explains that this is analogous to handshake, which is a sunnah inherited from early Muslims. The text cites al-Qunyah, which specifies that the sunnah form of handshake is using both hands.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Permissibility of Decade Markers and Diacritics on the Mushaf
Ruling on permissibility of Quranic aids

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adding markers and dots to the Mushaf and explain the basis for permissibility.

Core Idea
Adding decade markers and diacritical points to the Mushaf is permissible because it aids preservation and reading.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a practical question: is it allowed to add marks like verse counts and diacritical dots to the Quran? The text clarifies that such aids are not only permissible but commendable, as they help preserve the accurate recitation and understanding of the divine text. It also reconciles an apparent objection from early practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the text to determine the legal status (hukm) of adding ‘ushr markers and diacritical points. The phrase 'لا بأس' (there is no harm) indicates permissibility.
Hanafi jurists often use 'la ba's' to denote mubah or mandub.
2
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
Two rationales are given: preservation of verses and preservation of i'rab (grammatical endings). The text states these are 'hasan' (good) because they serve a necessary function.
The analogy is that just as the Quranic text is tawqifi (divinely fixed), aids that protect it from error are praiseworthy.
3
Address the counter-evidence
The report from Ibn Mas'ud 'Jarridu al-Quran' (strip the Quran) seems to oppose additions. The text explains that this was specific to their time when oral transmission was the norm.
This shows the principle of contextual interpretation of early rulings.
4
Extend the ruling to similar cases
The principle is extended: writing surah names and verse counts is also permissible, as they serve the same purpose of preservation.
This demonstrates qiyas (analogical reasoning) within the maslahah (benefit) framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعشير المصحف إلخ [هو أن يجعل على كل عشر آيات علامة. (عيني)] يعني يجوز؛ لأن القراءة والآية توقيفية ليس للرأي فيها مدخل، فالتعشير حفظ الآيات، والنقط حفظ الإعراب، فكانا حسنين؛ ولأن الأعجمي الذي لا يحفظ القرآن لا يقدر على القراءة إلا بالنقط، فكان حسنا وما روي عن ابن مسعود: "جردوا القرآن" فذلك في زمانهم؛ لأنهم كانوا ينقلونه عن النبي عليه كما أنزل، وعلى هذا لا بأس بكتابة أسامي السور وعد الآي
And 'ushr (decade marking) of the Mushaf etc. [It is to place a marker on every ten verses. (Ayni)] Means: it is permissible because the recitation and verses are tawqifi (divinely determined), and opinion has no entry in them; so 'ushr is a preservation of the verses, and diacritical points are preservation of the i'rab, so both are good. And because the non-Arab who does not memorize the Quran cannot read except by dots, so it is good. And what has been narrated from Ibn Mas'ud: "Strip the Quran" that was in their time, because they were transmitting it from the Prophet (peace be upon him) as it was revealed. On this basis, there is no harm in writing the names of the surahs and the count of verses.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of adding decade markers (ta'shir) and diacritical points (nuqat) to the Mushaf. It explains that these are permissible and even commendable (hasan) because they help preserve the accurate reading and understanding of the Quran, especially for non-Arabic speakers. The narration from Ibn Mas'ud instructing to 'strip the Quran' is contextualized to his time, when people had a direct oral transmission. In later times, such aids are beneficial. The same applies to writing surah names and verse counts.

Key Terms
التعشير (al-ta'shir): The practice of marking every ten verses with a symbol
النقط (al-nuqt): Diacritical points to distinguish letters and indicate vowels

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this text, why is adding diacritical points to the Quran permissible?
A) Because the Quran is not divine
B) Because it helps non-Arabs read correctly
C) Because Ibn Mas'ud commanded it
D) Because it is a required part of the revelation

Key Takeaway
Adding beneficial aids like decade markers and diacritical points to the Mushaf is permissible in the Hanafi school as a means of preserving correct recitation.

Entry of a Dhimmi into the Mosque: Hanafi View and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Permissibility of dhimmi entry: inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a dhimmi entering a mosque, explain the Hanafi proof from the Sunnah, and evaluate the opposing schools' dalil from Quran 9:28.

Core Idea
A dhimmi may enter any mosque because the Prophet hosted polytheists; their impurity is spiritual, not physical.

This lesson examines an important inter-madhhab dispute: may a non-Muslim (dhimmi) enter a mosque? The Hanafi school permits it unconditionally, while other schools restrict it. Understanding this dispute requires careful analysis of the primary texts: the sunnah of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the Quranic verse about polytheists' impurity. The Hanafi position, based on explicit practice, demonstrates that the impurity mentioned in the verse is spiritual, not physical.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: permissible (ja'iz) for a dhimmi to enter any mosque. This is the Hanafi default.
The text says 'جاز إدخال الذمي جميع المساجد عندنا'.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their views
Note that Malik dislikes entry into all mosques, Shafi'i restricts the Sacred Mosque only, and Ahmad agrees with Shafi'i. Malik extends the restriction by analogy.
Malik's extension is based on 'عموم العلة'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Quranic verse: 'Indeed the polytheists are unclean' (9:28). This is their primary dalil.
The verse explicitly uses the word 'najis'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis offer two responses: first, the Prophet hosted the delegation of Thaqif (polytheists) in the mosque. Second, Ali's statement clarifies that the impurity is spiritual ('on themselves'), not physical.
The sunnah action overrides a general interpretation of the verse.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in Hanafi practice, dhimmis may enter mosques freely; in other schools, they are restricted or prohibited.
This affects mosque management and interfaith relations today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخول ذمي إلخ: يعني جاز إدخال الذمي جميع المساجد عندنا وقال مالك يكره في كل المساجد، وقال الشافعي: يكره في المسجد الحرام؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّمَا الْمُشْرِكُونَ نَجَسٌ فَلا يَقْرَبُوا الْمَسْجِدَ الْحَرَامَ بَعْدَ عَامِهِمْ هَذَا (التوبة: 28) وبه قال أحمد، وعداه مالك إلى سائر المساجد؛ لعموم العلة، ولنا أن النبي عليه أنزل وفد ثقيف في المسجد، وضربت لهم خيمة فيه، فقالت الصحابة له: "المشركون نجس"، فقال علي: "ليس على الأرض من نجاستهم شيء، وإنما نجاستهم على أنفسهم."
And the entry of a dhimmi etc.: meaning it is permissible to admit a dhimmi into all mosques according to us. And Malik said it is disliked in all mosques. And al-Shafi‘i said it is disliked in the Sacred Mosque (al-Masjid al-Haram) due to His statement: 'Indeed the polytheists are unclean, so let them not approach the Sacred Mosque after this year' (al-Tawbah: 28). And Ahmad said the same, and Malik extended it to all mosques due to the generality of the cause (illah). And for us (Hanafis) is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) lodged the delegation of Thaqif in the mosque, and a tent was pitched for them in it. The Companions said: 'The polytheists are unclean.' 'Ali said: 'There is nothing on earth from their impurity; their impurity is only upon themselves.'
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents the inter-madhhab dispute over whether a dhimmi (non-Muslim resident) may enter a mosque. The Hanafi school permits entry into all mosques. They base this on the sunnah: the Prophet (peace be upon him) hosted the delegation of Thaqif (who were polytheists) inside the mosque. When some Companions expressed concern about impurity, 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) explained that their impurity is spiritual, not physical, so they do not physically contaminate the mosque. The opposing schools (Maliki, Shafi‘i, Hanbali) restrict entry based on the verse declaring polytheists as najis (unclean). Shafi‘i confines the restriction to the Sacred Mosque; Malik extends it to all mosques. The Hanafi view is strong because the sunnah (practice of the Prophet) takes precedence over a general interpretation of the verse.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Islamic protection with a covenant (dhimma)
نجس (najis): Ritually impure

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a dhimmi entering a mosque?
A) Permissible in all mosques
B) Disliked in all mosques
C) Prohibited in the Sacred Mosque only
D) Prohibited in all mosques

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits dhimmi entry into any mosque because the sunnah clarifies that the Quranic 'najis' refers to spiritual, not physical, impurity.

Visiting a Sick Dhimmi
Ruling with proof: visiting a sick dhimmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on visiting a sick dhimmi and explain the prophetic proof for its permissibility.

Core Idea
The Prophet visited his Jewish neighbor — visiting a sick dhimmi is permissible and recommended, not forbidden.

This lesson addresses the fiqh of visiting a sick non-Muslim, specifically a dhimmi. The Hanafi school permits and even recommends such visits, as demonstrated by the Prophet's own action. This ruling is based on a well-known hadith that also illustrates the prophetic approach to da'wah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act in question: visiting a sick dhimmi. Then identify the Hanafi hukm: mubah (permissible) and mustahabb (recommended).
The ruling is derived directly from the sunnah.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited in the text: the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) visited his Jewish neighbor and invited him to Islam.
The hadith is ahad but considered strong in meaning.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The Prophet's action (fi'l) indicates permissibility, and his explicit command (al-amr) 'let us visit' indicates recommendation (nadb).
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub unless qarinah to nadb exists here.
4
Note the cross-madhab status
Some scholars may restrict visiting to Muslims only, but Hanafis follow the prophetic example. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
The difference stems from interpreting general prohibitions versus specific actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعيادته يعني يجوز عيادة الذمي المريض؛ لما روي أن يهوديا مرض بجوار النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: "قوموا بنا نعود جارنا اليهودي"، فقاموا، ودخل النبي لها وقعد عند رأسه، وقال له: "قل: أشهد أن لا إله إلا الله، وأشهد أن محمدا عبده ورسوله"، فنظر المريض إلى أبيه، فقال: أجبه، فنطق بالشهادة، فقال : "الحمد لله الذي أنقذ نسمة بي من النار" الحديث
And visiting him: meaning it is permissible to visit a sick dhimmi; because it is narrated that a Jew fell ill near the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he said: "Get up, let us visit our Jewish neighbor." So they got up, and the Prophet entered upon him and sat at his head, and said to him: "Say: I bear witness that there is no god but Allah, and I bear witness that Muhammad is His servant and Messenger." The patient looked at his father, who said: "Answer him," so he uttered the testimony. Then the Prophet said: "Praise be to Allah who saved a soul from the Fire."
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of visiting a sick dhimmi. It is permissible and even recommended (mustahabb) based on the explicit action of the Prophet (peace be upon him), who visited a sick Jewish neighbor. This hadith also demonstrates the prophetic method of da'wah. The act is a form of birr (righteousness) to a neighbor, regardless of faith.

Key Terms
عيادة ('iyadah): Visiting the sick, a praiseworthy act

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He prayed for him from a distance

Key Takeaway
Visiting a sick dhimmi is permissible and recommended, as proven by the Prophet's own action.

Permissibility of Castrating Animals
Ruling on castrating animals with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on castrating animals and cite the prophetic precedent that supports it.

Core Idea
Castration is permissible because the Prophet sacrificed castrated rams, and it benefits the animal.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the permissibility of castrating animals. The author cites two proofs: the Prophet's own sacrifice of castrated rams and the principle that causing pain for a benefit to the animal is allowed. We will examine these proofs and the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is khisa', castration of animals. Identify that the ruling stated is 'ja'iz' (permissible).
The author says 'yajuzu' clearly.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The primary proof is the Prophet's action: he sacrificed two castrated rams. This is a hadith.
The term 'mawju'' means castrated.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's sacrificing castrated rams implies the permissibility of castration because sacrifices must be from permissible sources.
If castration were forbidden, the Prophet would not have offered them.
4
Note the principle from al-Muhit
The text quotes al-Muhit: causing pain to an animal is allowed if there is a benefit that returns to the animal itself (like better meat, preventing harm).
This distinguishes castration from mutilation.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is permissibility.
No ikhtilaf among Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخصاء البهائم: من خصيت الفرس خصيا وخصاءا إذا نزعت بيضته يعني يجوز خصاء البهائم؛ لأنه عليها ضحى بكبشين أملحين موجوئين والموجوء هو الخصي؛ ولأن فيه تطييب لحمه، وترك النطاح والشر، فكان حسنا، وفي "المحيط": أن الأصل إيصال الألم إلى الحيوان لمصلحة تعود إلى الحيوان يجوز ، ولا بأس بكي البهائم للعلامة، ولا بأس بكي الصبيان إذا كان لداء تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤١٠/٨] (وغيره) وإنزاء الحمير إلخ [من النزو وهو الوثب كناية عن الجماع] يعني جاز إنزاء الحمير على الخيل؛ لأنه علي ركب البغل واقتناه، ولو حرم لما فعل تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤١٣/٨]
And castrating animals: from 'khasaytu al-faras' (I castrated the horse) if you removed its testicles. Meaning it is permissible to castrate animals because the Prophet sacrificed two white-and-black castrated rams (mawju'ayn), and 'mawju'' means castrated. Also because it improves the meat and prevents butting and evil, so it is good. In al-Muhit: the basic principle is that causing pain to an animal for a benefit that returns to the animal is permissible. There is no harm in branding animals for marking, and no harm in cauterizing children if it is for a disease. (Tatimmah al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:410) And mounting donkeys upon horses etc.: from 'al-nazw' meaning jumping, a metaphor for copulation. Meaning it is permissible to mount donkeys upon horses because Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) rode a mule and owned it, and if it were forbidden he would not have done it. (Tatimmah al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:413)
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings. First, the permissibility of castrating animals (khisa'). The proof is that the Prophet sacrificed castrated rams, which indicates its permissibility. Benefits include better meat and preventing fighting. The principle is that causing pain to an animal is allowed if there is a benefit that returns to the animal itself (like health, temperament). This is different from mutilation. Also, branding animals for identification and cauterizing children for medical reasons are allowed under the same principle. Second, it is permissible to mate donkeys with horses (to produce mules). The proof is that 'Ali owned and rode a mule (a hybrid of horse and donkey), and if that were forbidden he would not have done so. The mule's existence itself indicates permissibility of the mating.

Key Terms
موجوء (mawju'): Castrated; an animal whose testicles have been removed.
خصاء (khisa'): Castration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary proof for the permissibility of castrating animals in the Hanafi school?
A) The Quranic verse on animal sacrifice.
B) The Prophet's sacrifice of castrated rams.
C) The consensus of the Companions.
D) Qiyas on marriage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits castrating animals when there is a benefit to the animal, proven by the Prophet's sacrifice of castrated rams.

Permissibility of Crossbreeding Horses and Donkeys (Mules)
Ruling on crossbreeding horses and donkeys with proof from Ali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breeding horses with donkeys and explain the proof from the action of Ali.

Core Idea
Breeding horses with donkeys is permissible because Ali rode a mule, and he would not do what is forbidden.

The second part of the passage addresses the permissibility of mating donkeys with horses to produce mules. The proof is the practice of Ali ibn Abi Talib, who owned and rode a mule. The author argues that if it were forbidden, Ali would not have done so. We will examine this proof and the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is inzal al-himar 'ala al-khayl (mounting a donkey on a horse), i.e., crossbreeding. The ruling is 'ja'iz' (permissible).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the action of Ali: he rode a mule and owned it.
A mule is the offspring of a horse and a donkey.
3
Understand the logical implication
Ali is a respected Companion. If crossbreeding were forbidden, he would not have engaged in it. Therefore, it must be permissible.
This is a form of istidlal bi fi'l al-sahabi.
4
Note the absence of contrary proof
No evidence prohibits crossbreeding; the action of Ali establishes permissibility.
5
State the mufta bihi
The operative ruling is permissibility.
No ikhtilaf among Hanafis.

This is different from mutilation. Also, branding animals for identification and cauterizing children for medical reasons are allowed under the same principle. The proof is that 'Ali owned and rode a mule (a hybrid of horse and donkey), and if that were forbidden he would not have done so. The mule's existence itself indicates permissibility of the mating.

Ruling on Using Eunuchs: Makruh Due to Encouraging Prohibited Mutilation
Ruling statement with proof: dislike of employing eunuchs due to inciting castration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on employing eunuchs, explain the evidence from the prohibition of muthlah, and distinguish between the general and restricted scope of the dislike.

Core Idea
Employing eunuchs is disliked because it normalizes castration, a form of muthlah (mutilation) prohibited by the Prophet.

The Hanafi school addresses the employment of eunuchs not merely as a matter of social custom but as a legal issue linked to the prohibition of muthlah (mutilation). This passage explores the ruling, its proof, and the scope of the dislike.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is employing eunuchs. The ruling is makruh (disliked). Note: some say haram because it encourages haram.
The ruling is based on indirect encouragement.
2
Locate the dalil
The Prophet prohibited muthlah (mutilation). Castration is muthlah. So, anything that encourages castration is at least makruh.
The hadith is ahad, making the evidence zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Employing eunuchs makes castration seem normal, thus indirectly promoting a prohibited act. The hadith 'نهى عن المثلة' is the proof.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Is the dislike general (any service) or specific (entering the women's quarters)? The 'asahh' view restricts to entering harem; al-Zayla'i holds general dislike.
Both views are valid within the madhhab.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that it is at least makruh; some later scholars lean toward prohibition (haram) due to the strength of the prohibition of muthlah.
Taqwa (piety) suggests avoiding employment altogether.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستخدام الخصي إلخ : أي يكره استخدامه؛ لأن فيه تحريض الناس على الخصي ، وهو مثلة، وقد نهى عنه النبي فتحرم. تكملة البحر الرائق: ٤١٤/٨] وإطلاق قوله : واستخدام الخصي" يشير إلى أن مطلق الخدمة مكروه، وذكر في الأصح إنما يكره استخدامهم في الخدمة المعهودة، وهو الدخول في الحريم، وعلى الإطلاق جرى الزيلعي لتعليله بقوله؛ لأن فيه تحريض الناس على الخصاء، وهو مثلة. And using eunuchs etc.: meaning it is disliked to use them because it incites people to castration, which is mutilation (muthlah), and the Prophet prohibited it, so it is forbidden. (Tatimmah al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 8:414) And the unrestricted wording of his statement: 'and using eunuchs' indicates that any form of service is disliked. It is mentioned in al-Asahh that it is only disliked to use them for the usual service, which is entering the women's quarters. But al-Zayla'i held the general dislike due to his reasoning: 'because it incites people to castration, which is mutilation.' (Fath)
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage states that it is disliked (makruh) to employ eunuchs (khasi) for service. The reason is that employing a eunuch normalizes or encourages the practice of castration, which is mutilation (muthlah) and has been prohibited by the Prophet. The term 'muthlah' refers to disfiguring or mutilating the body, which is a major sin. There is a discussion on the scope: the unrestricted wording suggests any service by eunuchs is disliked. However, in the 'asahh' (more correct) view, the dislike is specifically for services that involve entering the women's quarters (harem), because that is the common service where eunuchs were used. Al-Zayla'i favored the general dislike based on the reasoning of incitement. The author leaves both views, but the ruling is that it is at least makruh to employ eunuchs for typical servitude.

Key Terms
مثلة (muthlah): Mutilation or disfigurement of the body, especially of limbs or genitals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Ijma of the Companions.
D) Qiyas on intoxicants.

Key Takeaway
Employing eunuchs is disliked (makruh) because it encourages castration, a prohibited form of mutilation (muthlah), with an intra-Hanafi difference on the scope of the dislike.

Ruling on the Supplication 'بمعقد العز من عرشك'
Dislike of the supplication due to anthropomorphic implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the supplication 'بمعقد العز من عرشك' and evaluate the differing views using rational and transmitted evidence.

Core Idea
A supplication that implies anthropomorphism is makruh tahrimi, even if used by a Companion.

This lesson examines a specific supplication that has two possible wordings. One wording clearly implies sitting (like a human), which is impossible for Allah. The other implies that Allah's glory is tied to the Throne, which also suggests limitation. Some early Hanafi imams permitted it based on the practice of 'Ali, but later scholars disliked it due to the danger of anthropomorphic implication. We will explore the legal reasoning and the intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible wordings
Note the two roots: 'aqada (to tie) and qa'ada (to sit). The author states the second is clearly disliked.
The first wording is also problematic.
2
Understand the rational argument for dislike
The phrase suggests that Allah's glory is attached to the Throne, which is a creation. Since the Throne is created, anything attached to it would also be created, but Allah's glory is eternal.
This implies a deficiency in Allah's attribute.
3
Note the transmitted evidence that permits it
Abu Yusuf and the 'three' (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and another) permitted it because 'Ali (ra) used it.
This becomes a point of ikhtilaf.
4
Evaluate which view is relied upon
The author leans towards dislike (makruh tahrimi) due to the rational risk of tashbih, even though some early authorities allowed it.
Later Hanafi scholars consider it makruh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمعقد العز إلخ يروى بعبارتين: الأولى من العقد، والثانية من القعود ولا شك في كراهية الثانية؛ لاستحالة معناها على الله تعالى، وكذا الأولى؛ لأنها توهم تعلق عزه بالعرش، والعرش حادث، وما تعلق به بهذا الوجه يكون حادثا، وعز الله قديم لا ينفك عنه أزلا وأبدا، وقال أبو يوسف: لا بأس به؛ لأنه علي دعا به، وبه قالت الثلاثة،
With 'the knot of glory (mu'qad al-'izz) from Your Throne' etc. It is narrated in two expressions: the first from 'al-'aqd' (tying/knot) and the second from 'al-qu'ud' (sitting). There is no doubt about the dislike of the second because its meaning is impossible for Allah, and likewise the first because it suggests that His glory is attached to the Throne, and the Throne is created, and whatever is attached in this manner is created, and the glory of Allah is eternal and never separate from Him pre-eternally and everlastingly. Abu Yusuf said: There is nothing wrong with it because 'Ali prayed with it, and the three (imams) also said so.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on the supplication 'بمعقد العز من عرشك' (literally: by the knot of glory from Your Throne). The phrase can be read in two ways: 'mu'qad al-'izz' from 'aqada (to tie) or 'muq'ad al-'izz' from qa'ada (to sit). The second reading (pertaining to sitting) is clearly disliked (makruh) because it attributes sitting to Allah, which is anthropomorphic and impossible. The first reading (knot of glory) is also disliked because it suggests that Allah's glory (al-'izz) is tied to the Throne, implying that His glory is dependent on a creation, which contradicts His eternality. However, Abu Yusuf holds that there is no harm in this supplication because 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) used it. The 'three' (likely Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and another) also allowed it. So there is a difference: the author (and possibly the later Hanafis) dislike it due to the incorrect implication, while early authorities like Abu Yusuf permitted it based on precedent.

Key Terms
توهم (tawahhum): Suggesting or implying a false meaning; in this context, anthropomorphism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the supplication 'بمعقد العز من عرشك' considered disliked by many Hanafi scholars?
A) Because it contains a word that is not Arabic.
B) Because it implies that Allah's glory is tied to the created Throne.
C) Because it was not said by the Prophet.
D) Because it is too long for supplication.

Key Takeaway
Supplications that imply anthropomorphism are avoided in Hanafi fiqh due to the principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Permissibility of Castrating Animals and Related Practices
Rulings on animal treatment and cross-breeding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on castrating animals, branding, cauterization, and covering donkeys on horses, and identify the proofs from prophetic action and benefit.

Core Idea
Castration, branding, and cross-breeding are permissible if they bring benefit.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with practices that may raise concerns about causing pain or altering creation. The author clarifies that castrating animals, branding them for identification, cauterizing children for medical reasons, and covering donkeys on horses to produce mules are all permissible. The rulings are grounded in prophetic precedent (fi'l) and the principle of benefit (maslahah). Understanding these rulings helps you apply the Hanafi method of deriving permissibility from the Prophet's actions and from the objective of achieving benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts being ruled upon
The passage covers four practices: (1) castrating animals, (2) branding animals, (3) cauterizing children, and (4) covering donkeys on horses (i.e., cross-breeding to produce mules). Note each.
2
Extract the ruling for each act
The author states each is permissible (ja’iz). For castration, he gives two proofs; for the others, he gives brief reasons.
3
Analyze the first proof for castration: prophetic example
The text cites that the Prophet (peace be upon him) sacrificed castrated rams. This is a fi'l (action) of the Prophet, indicating permissibility unless there is evidence to the contrary.
The hadith is considered sahih (al-Bukhari and Muslim).
4
Analyze the second proof: benefit (maslahah)
Castration improves the meat (makes it tender) and prevents fighting (butting and harm). The benefit returns to the animal, so the pain is outweighed.
The text cites al-Muhit for the principle that causing pain for the animal's benefit is permissible.
5
Apply the same logic to the other practices
Branding for identification and cauterization for treatment are permissible because they serve a clear benefit. Covering donkeys on horses was done by the Prophet (he rode and kept a mule), so his action establishes permissibility.
For cross-breeding, the Prophet's mule is a result of such a cross, so it is implicitly approved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية
وخصاء البهائم: من خصيت الفرس خصيا وخصاءا إذا نزعت بيضته يعني يجوز خصاء البهائم؛ لأنه عليه ضحى بكبشين أملحين موجوئين والموجوء هو الخصي؛ ولأن فيه تطييب لحمه، وترك النطاح والشر، فكان حسنا، وفي "المحيط": أن الأصل إيصال الألم إلى الحيوان لمصلحة تعود إلى الحيوان يجوز ، ولا بأس بكي البهائم للعلامة، ولا بأس بكي الصبيان إذا كان لداء (وغيره) وإنزاء الحمير إلخ [من النزو وهو الوثب كناية عن الجماع] يعني جاز إنزاء الحمير على الخيل؛ لأنه عليه ركب البغل واقتناه، ولو حرم لما فعل.
The Book of Disliked Acts. And castrating animals: from 'you castrate the horse' (khasayta) meaning it is permissible to castrate animals; because the Prophet (peace be upon him) sacrificed two horned, castrated rams, and the castrated one is the one whose testicles are removed; and because it improves the meat and prevents butting and evil, so it is good. In al-Muhit: The principle is that causing pain to an animal for a benefit that returns to the animal is permissible. And there is no harm in branding animals for identification, and no harm in cauterizing children if it is for a disease. (And other) Covering donkeys on horses: from al-nazw meaning jumping, as a metaphor for mating. It means it is permissible to cover donkeys on horses; because the Prophet (peace be upon him) rode a mule and kept it, and if it were forbidden, he would not have done so.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings on practices involving animals and humans. It begins by confirming the permissibility of castrating animals (khisa' al-baha'im). The proof is twofold: first, the Prophet himself sacrificed castrated rams, indicating approval; second, castration improves the meat and prevents harmful fighting among animals, so it serves a clear benefit. The commentator cites al-Muhit to establish the general principle that inflicting pain on an animal is permissible when the benefit returns to the animal itself. Next, the text rules that branding animals for identification is permissible, as is cauterizing children for medical treatment. Finally, the passage covers the case of covering donkeys on horses (inza' al-humur 'ala al-khayl) to produce mules. This is deemed permissible because the Prophet rode a mule and kept one, and his action indicates permissibility. The ruling is based on prophetic precedent (fi'l) as dalil naqli.

Key Terms
خصاء (khiṣā’): Castration of an animal by removing the testicles.
كي (kayy): Branding with fire (cauterization).
إنزاء (inzā’): Covering (i.e., mating) a donkey on a horse to produce a mule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Hanafis permit castration, branding, cauterization, and cross-breeding when they serve a clear benefit, supported by prophetic action.

Rulings on Accepting Gifts and Using Eunuchs from a Slave Merchant
Rulings on gifts, invitations, borrowing, and eunuch use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting gifts from a slave merchant, distinguish between permissible and disliked categories, and explain the prohibition of using eunuchs.

Core Idea
Istihsan permits accepting a slave's gift, but valuable items are makruh; using eunuchs is haram.

This passage addresses interactions with a slave who is a merchant. It clarifies that while general analogy (qiyas) would prohibit accepting gifts from a slave because he cannot donate, the Hanafi school applies istihsan based on prophetic practice. However, caution is advised for valuable gifts. Additionally, the use of eunuchs as servants is strongly discouraged due to the prohibition of castration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their initial ruling by qiyas
First, recognize the acts: accepting gifts, answering invitations, borrowing beasts, and using eunuchs. By strict qiyas, these are not permissible because a slave lacks legal capacity to donate or lend.
Qiyas here means the general principle that a slave's property belongs to his master.
2
Apply istihsan based on prophetic practice
The Hanafi school overrides qiyas with istihsan, citing the hadith that the Prophet accepted a gift from Salman al-Farisi while he was still a slave, and he answered a slave's invitation.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi source of law; it allows a departure from qiyas due to stronger evidence or necessity.
3
Distinguish between small and valuable gifts
While accepting gifts is generally permissible by istihsan, it is disliked (makruh) to accept valuable items like garments or coins because the slave is not fully qualified to donate large amounts.
The text explicitly mentions 'kareh kuswatahu' (disliked to accept his clothing) and similar for dirhams/dinars.
4
Identify the ruling on using eunuchs
Using a eunuch (khasī) as a servant is disliked to the point of prohibition because it encourages castration, which is mutilation forbidden by the Prophet.
The text states 'tahrim' (forbidden) due to the hadith prohibiting castration.
5
Summarize the overall Hanafi position
The Hanafi position is that dealings with a slave merchant are permitted by istihsan, but with caution: small gifts are allowed, valuable ones are makruh, and using eunuchs is haram.
The mufta bihi is the permissibility of gifts and invitations, but with the above qualifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبول هدية العبد إلخ: لأن ذلك من ضرورات التجارة، وقد صح أن سلمان الفارسي له أهدى إلى النبي هدية قبل أن يعتق، فقبلها النبي وكان يجيب دعوة المملوك. وهذا هو الاستحسان والقياس أن لا يجوز؛ لأنه تبرع، والعبد ليس من أهله، لكن جوز ما ذكر لما ذكرنا. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٨٥/٢] و كره كسوته إلخ يعني كره كسوة العبد التاجر الثوب أي قبول هديته إذا كانت ثوبا، وكذا كره هديته الدراهم والدنانير؛ لأنه تبرع، والعبد ليس من أهله، ولكن جوز في الشيء اليسير للضرورة استحسانا كما مر. واستخدام الخصي إلخ : أي يكره استخدامه؛ لأن فيه تحريض الناس على الخصي ، وهو مثلة، وقد نهى عنه النبي فتحرم.
And accepting the gift of the slave etc: because that is from the necessities of trade, and it is authentically reported that Salman al-Farisi (may Allah be pleased with him) gave a gift to the Prophet (peace be upon him) before he was freed, and the Prophet accepted it, and he used to answer the invitation of a slave. This is istihsan, and qiyas is that it is not permissible because it is a voluntary donation and the slave is not qualified, but what was mentioned is permitted for what we mentioned. And it is disliked to accept his clothing etc: meaning it is disliked to accept the slave merchant's gift of a garment, and likewise it is disliked to accept his gift of silver and gold coins; because it is a donation and the slave is not qualified, but a small amount is permitted out of necessity by istihsan as previously. And using the eunuch etc: meaning it is disliked to use him because that encourages people to castration, which is mutilation, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade it, so it is forbidden.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings regarding a slave who is a merchant (al-'abd al-tajir). The first ruling: it is permissible to accept a gift from such a slave, to answer his invitation, and to borrow his beast. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) despite qiyas (analogy) suggesting otherwise. The proof is the prophetic precedent: the Prophet accepted a gift from Salman al-Farisi while Salman was still a slave, and he used to accept a slave's invitation. The second ruling: it is disliked (makruh) to accept a gift of a garment or cash from the slave, because that is a donation and the slave is not fully qualified to give. However, a small amount is allowed out of necessity. The third ruling: using a eunuch (khasī) as a servant is disliked, because it encourages people to castrate, which is mutilation. The Prophet forbade castration, so it becomes haram (prohibited).

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that allows a ruling contrary to qiyas due to necessity or stronger evidence.
خصي (khaṣī): A eunuch, a castrated male slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is using a eunuch as a servant disliked?
A) Because it is a form of charity.
B) Because it encourages castration.
C) Because eunuchs are impure.
D) Because it wastes money.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan permits accepting a slave merchant's gift, but valuable items are disliked; using eunuchs is prohibited due to castration.
